Implication of an Absolute Command: Intrinsic or Instrumental Goodness?
Explaining two Hanafi views on the implication of an absolute command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two Hanafi positions on what an absolute command indicates regarding goodness, and apply the proof from necessity to derive the minimal implication.

Core Idea
An absolute command necessarily implies goodness, but only the minimal degree (hasan li-ghayrihi) unless a qarina elevates it.

In Hanafi usul, an absolute command (al-amr al-mutlaq) is a command without any contextual indicator specifying the nature of the commanded act's goodness. Our passage presents two views: one that it indicates intrinsic goodness (hasan li-dhatihi), and another that it indicates instrumental goodness (hasan li-ghayrihi). We will examine the proof for the latter, based on the wisdom of the Commander and the principle of necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Locate the two views mentioned: Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi (hasan li-dhatihi) and the author of Badi' al-Nizam (hasan li-ghayrihi). Note that both are within the Hanafi madhhab.
2
Understand the proof for the second view
The author provides a rational proof: the Commander is wise, so He does not command evil; therefore every commanded act must contain some good. But because the command is absolute (no qarina), we cannot assume more than the minimum necessary good.
This proof relies on the attribute of wisdom (hikmah) and the principle of necessity (darurah).
3
Analyze the logical structure
Break down the proof: (1) The Commander is wise. (2) A wise commander only commands good. (3) Goodness necessarily exists in the command. (4) Since the command is absolute, we take the minimal degree (hasan li-ghayrihi).
The key is that 'minimal' is justified by 'necessity'.
4
Consider the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if a command is absolute, is the act considered intrinsically good (like prayer) or merely instrumentally good (like writing a will for a worldly purpose)? This affects analogical reasoning and reward calculation.
For example, an absolute command to 'give charity' might be fulfilled by giving to gain a reputation, not purely for Allah.
5
Relate to other usul principles
Note how this dispute connects to the broader usul discussion on commands: whether al-amr yufidu al-wujub (obligation) and whether it implies hasan (goodness).
The second view aligns with caution: do not attribute more to the command than necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ماذا يفيد الأمر المطلق الحسن) لعينه أو الحسن لغيره ؟ وذكر المصنف فيه مذهبين : مذهب شمس الأئمة السرخسي، ومذهب الشاعاتي في كتابه "بديع النظام " وقال: الأمر المطلق المجرد (الخالى) عن القرينة (الدالة على أحد الحسنين)
١ - هل (يكون ) للحسن لنفسه (الذى) لا يقبل السقوط؟ كما اختاره شمس الأئمة (السرخسي)
٢ - أو يكون للحسن لغيره : كما في "بديع النظام ؟
ثم ذكر المصنف دليل صاحب "البديع " وقال : لثبوت الحسن في المأمور به اقتضاة، فثبت (بقدر الضرورة) الأدنى منه، وهو الحسن لغيره .
وإنما يثبت الحسن في المأمور به اقتضاة لأن الأمر حكيم، وهو لا يأمر بالفحشاء والمنكر، وإنما يأمر بما فيه الحسن ضرورة، فيتقدر بقدر الضرورة، وتتحقق الضرورة بأدنى مرتبة الحسن، وهو الحسن لغيره .
What does the absolute command indicate: goodness in itself or goodness for something else? The author mentioned two schools: the school of Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi, and the school of al-Shami in his book 'Badi' al-Nizam'. He said: The absolute command, stripped of any indicator (pointing to one of the two goods): 1. Is it for goodness in itself that does not accept removal? As chosen by Shams al-A'imma (al-Sarakhsi). 2. Or is it for goodness for something else? As in 'Badi' al-Nizam'. Then the author mentioned the proof of the author of 'al-Badi'' and said: Because goodness in the commanded act is established by necessity, so its minimum degree is established, which is goodness for something else. Goodness is only established in the commanded act by necessity because the Commander is wise, and He does not command indecency and evil, but only commands that in which there is goodness necessarily. Thus it is limited to the extent of necessity, and necessity is realized with the minimum degree of goodness, which is goodness for something else.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage addresses a key usul dispute about the nature of goodness indicated by an absolute command (al-amr al-mutlaq) — a command without any contextual evidence specifying whether the commanded act is good in itself or good for something else. Two positions are presented: (1) Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi holds that such a command indicates goodness in itself (hasan li-dhatihi), which cannot cease; (2) The author of 'Badi' al-Nizam' (likely al-Shami or al-Shatibi) holds that it indicates goodness for something else (hasan li-ghayrihi) — the minimal degree. The proof for the second view is based on necessity: the Commander (Allah) is wise and does not command evil; therefore, the commanded act must contain some goodness. However, since the command is absolute, we cannot assume more than the minimum necessary, which is that the act is good for something else. This has practical implications: for example, a command without qualification may be fulfilled by an act that is instrumentally good (e.g., acquiring knowledge for a worldly benefit) rather than intrinsically good (e.g., prayer). The Hanafi school generally relies on such rational analysis to determine the implications of divine commands.

Key Terms
الأمر المطلق (al-amr al-muṭlaq): A command devoid of any contextual indicator (qarina) specifying the nature of the commanded act's goodness.
الحسن لعينه (al-ḥasan li-ʿaynihī): Goodness intrinsic to the act itself, which does not cease or depend on external factors.
الحسن لغيره (al-ḥasan li-ghayrihī): Goodness that comes from an external benefit or purpose, not from the act's own essence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the proof for the view in 'Badi' al-Nizam', why does an absolute command indicate hasan li-ghayrihi rather than hasan li-dhatihi?
A) Because the Commander is merciful
B) Because goodness must be established by necessity, and the minimum degree is hasan li-ghayrihi
C) Because all commands are for worldly benefit
D) Because hasan li-dhatihi is impossible

Key Takeaway
An absolute command necessarily implies some good, but only the minimal degree (hasan li-ghayrihi) is established unless a contextual indicator specifies otherwise.

The Terminological Nature of the Dispute on Wad'i Rulings
Response to objection about adding 'wad'an'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why the Hanafi school does not add 'wad'an' to the definition of hukm and evaluate whether the dispute is substantive or terminological.

Core Idea
The disagreement over adding 'wad'an' is merely terminological, not substantive.

In Hanafi usul, the definition of 'hukm' is reserved for taklifi rulings (obligation, prohibition, etc.). Some scholars objected that this excludes wad'i rulings (cause, condition, impediment) and proposed adding 'wad'an' to the definition. This passage presents the Hanafi response: wad'i rulings are not called 'ahkam' in the technical sense, so no qualifier is needed. The dispute is one of terminology, not legal substance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue in dispute
Recognise that the objector wants to include wad'i rulings in the definition of 'hukm' by adding the qualifier 'wad'an'. The Hanafi position is that wad'i rulings are not part of the technical definition.
2
State the objector's argument
The objector argues that the definition 'the address of Allah related to the actions of the responsible person' excludes wad'i rulings, which relate to causes not actions, so the definition is incomplete without 'wad'an'.
3
Examine the Hanafi response
The Hanafis respond that they simply do not call wad'i rulings 'ahkam' in the technical sense. This is not a denial of their existence but a terminological choice.
The text says: 'We do not call them rulings, even if others call them rulings.'
4
Recognise the terminological nature
Both sides acknowledge the same categories of rulings; the difference is only in naming. The phrase 'there is no contention in terminology' confirms the dispute is not substantive.
5
Determine the practical consequence
If 'wad'an' were added, the term 'hukm' would include both taklifi and wad'i. Hanafis keep it restricted to taklifi, treating wad'i as a separate category. This affects how usul principles are applied, but both approaches are valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجواب الثاني من جانب من لا يحس بضرورة زيادة قيد "وضعاً" وتارة يمنع (من لم يزد قيد (وضعاً) كونها (كون الأحكام الوضعية من المحدود ( من الحكم المعزف بفتح الراء) (وقال في دليله) فانا لا نسميها أحكاماً، وإن سماها غيرنا أحكاماً ( وهذا اصطلاح) ولا مشاحة (لابخل والمضايقة في الاصطلاح).
The second answer from the side of those who do not feel the need to add the qualifier 'wad'an': sometimes they deny that the wad'i rulings are part of the defined (the definition of hukm). They say in their proof: 'We do not call them rulings, even if others call them rulings. And this is a matter of terminology, and there is no contention in terminology.'
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents a response to an objection that the definition of hukm should include the qualifier 'wad'an' to cover the wad'i rulings (such as cause, condition, impediment). The objector argues that the definition of hukm as 'the address of Allah related to the actions of the responsible person' excludes wad'i rulings because they are not directly related to actions but to causes. The answer here is from those who do not add 'wad'an': they either deny that wad'i rulings are part of the defined term 'hukm' at all, or they consider it a mere terminological difference. They state that they do not call wad'i rulings 'ahkam' in the technical sense, even if others do. Therefore, there is no need to add the qualifier, and the disagreement is only about naming. The phrase 'no contention in terminology' indicates that as long as the meaning is clear, the term itself is not a serious dispute.

Key Terms
قيد وضعاً (qayd wad'an): The qualifier 'wad'an' added to the definition of hukm to include wad'i rulings.
الحكم الوضعي (al-hukm al-wad'i): A ruling that is not directly about obligation but about causation, condition, or impediment.
اصطلاح (istilah): Technical terminology; the disagreement is merely terminological.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do some scholars not add 'wad'an' to the definition of hukm?
A) They deny the existence of wad'i rulings entirely.
B) They do not consider wad'i rulings as part of the technical term 'hukm'.
C) They believe the qualifier is grammatically incorrect.
D) They follow a different definition from other schools.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's refusal to add 'wad'an' is a terminological choice, reflecting that the technical term 'hukm' is reserved for taklifi rulings, while wad'i rulings are acknowledged separately.

The Eternity of Divine Discourse: Khitab in Usul al-Fiqh
Analyzing the definition of divine discourse and its eternal status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define khitab according to the Hanafi school, explain the dispute over its eternity, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf regarding the temporal status of divine rulings.

Core Idea
The eternal attribute of divine speech makes khitab eternal; the ruling (hukm) is therefore eternal, not temporal.

This passage tackles a foundational usul al-fiqh dispute: does divine discourse (khitab) exist eternally in the divine attribute of speech, or does it arise only when addressed to creation? The answer shapes how we understand the nature of Allah's commands and whether obligations like prayer are eternal decrees or temporal utterances. Let's analyze the two definitions and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Definitions
The passage presents two interpretations of khitab. The first: speech that conveys its meaning, regardless of an addressee. The second: speech that actually causes understanding in the past or present, requiring an addressee.
Note the key phrase 'يفهم معناه' (conveys its meaning) vs. 'أفهم' (caused understanding).
2
Understand the Hanafi Position
The Hanafi school (and the author who calls it 'the truth') adopts the first definition. This makes khitab eternal because the attribute of speech is eternal and meaning is inherent in the speech itself.
This relies on the concept of kalam nafsi (internal speech) as an eternal attribute.
3
Understand the Opposing Position
The Mutakallimin/Ash'ari position requires an actual addressee for something to be called khitab. Since no addressees existed in eternity, khitab is temporal, occurring only when addressees exist.
This view ties khitab to the act of addressing, not just the potential for meaning.
4
Identify the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The dispute affects whether the hukm (ruling) of kalam nafsi is eternal or temporal. If khitab is eternal, the ruling (e.g., obligation to pray) is an eternal decree. If temporal, the ruling arises only when addressed to humans in time.
This has implications for the binding force of commands before revelation.
5
Note the Tarjih
The author explicitly states 'والحق' (and the truth) for the first definition, indicating the preferred Hanafi view. The tarjih is based on comprehensiveness: the first definition does not depend on the contingent existence of an addressee.
Tarjih is not fully argued here but is asserted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض، ثم اشار إلى الجواب بقوله : "والحق أنه (أن الخطاب إن فسر بما (بالكلام الذي) يفهم معناه (في الحال والمآل) كان الكلام خطاباً فيه (في الأزل وفيهما لايزال) أي في الماضي الذي لا ابتداء له، وفي المستقبل الذي لا نهاية له. وإن فسر الخطاب بما بالكلام الذي أفهم أي أوقع به الفهم في الماضي أو الحال لم يكن الكلام خطاباً في الأزل لعدم وجود المخاطبين في الأزل، بل يكون الكلام خطاباً فيما لا يزال في المستقبل غير المتناهي) عند وجود المخاطبين. ويبتني عليه (على هذا الخلاف) أنه (أن الكلام النفسي) حكم في الأزل أو فيما لا يزال، فمن يقول إن الكلام النفسي خطاب أزلي، يقول: إن الكلام النفسي أيضاً حكم أزلي، ومن قال إنه (الكلام) خطاب فيما لا يزال، قال: الكلام النفسي أيضاً حكم فيما لا يزال."
He said: 'And the truth is that if discourse is interpreted as (the speech) that conveys its meaning (immediately and eventually), then the speech is discourse in (eternity and forever) i.e. in the past without beginning and in the future without end. And if discourse is interpreted as the speech that caused understanding in the past or present, then the speech is not discourse in eternity due to the absence of addresses in eternity; rather the speech is discourse forever (in the endless future) when addressees exist. And upon this dispute depends whether (the internal speech) is a ruling in eternity or forever. So whoever says that internal speech is eternal discourse says that internal speech is also an eternal ruling. And whoever says that it is discourse forever says that internal speech is also a ruling forever.'
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage addresses a key dispute in usul al-fiqh: the definition of khitab (divine address) and its temporal scope. The author presents two interpretations. The first defines khitab as speech that conveys meaning, regardless of the presence of an addressee; on this view, the internal speech (kalam nafsi) is an eternal discourse and thus the ruling (hukm) attaching to it is eternal. The second defines khitab as speech that actually causes understanding, which only occurs when an addressee exists; on that view, the ruling is temporal. The Hanafi school adopts the first position, holding that Allah's attribute of speech is eternal and that the ruling is likewise eternal. This disagreement has significant implications for the nature of divine command and obligation.

Key Terms
خطاب (khitab): Divine discourse; speech that conveys a meaning to an addressee.
كلام نفسي (kalam nafsi): Internal speech; an eternal attribute of Allah that is not composed of letters or sounds.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) of the dispute over the definition of khitab?
A) Whether the Quran is created or uncreated.
B) Whether internal speech (kalam nafsi) and its ruling are eternal or temporal.
C) Whether Allah speaks with letters and sounds.
D) Whether the Prophet received revelation directly or through an angel.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi view that khitab is defined as speech conveying meaning (regardless of addressee) leads to the conclusion that divine rulings are eternal attributes inherent in Allah's speech.

The Nature of Fard Kifayah: Individual Obligation or Ambiguous Group?
Usul dispute: nature of obligation in fard kifayah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and contrast the two positions on the obligation in fard kifayah, apply the Hanafi argument to determine the ruling when a communal obligation is abandoned, and evaluate the opposing view's analogical reasoning.

Core Idea
Fard kifayah obligates every individual, but the act of some lifts it from all.

This lesson dives into a foundational usul dispute: when the Lawgiver commands a communal obligation (fard kifayah), who exactly is bound? Is the obligation on every single individual, or does it target an unspecified part of the community? The answer has profound implications for sin and responsibility. We will unpack the Hanafi view and its response to the opposing school, using the text's own proofs and analogies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Issue
Read the opening line of the passage carefully. The issue is about the nature of obligation in fard kifayah: is it on every individual (wajib 'ala kulli wahid) or on an unspecified part (al-ba'd al-mubham)? Note that both sides agree that performance by some suffices; the disagreement is about on whom the original duty is placed.
The term 'ibham al-mukallaf' (ambiguity of the obliged) is central to the dispute.
2
State the Hanafi Position and its Proof
The Hanafi/majority position is that the obligation is on each and every individual. The passage gives a key proof: if everyone abandons the act knowing that no one else did it, they all sin. This proves the duty is on each. Note also the examples of funeral prayer and jihad.
The fact that sin attaches to all if all neglect it is the cornerstone of the argument.
3
Identify the Opposing View and its Proofs
The opposing school argues the obligation is on an unspecified part. Their first proof: the obligation falls by the act of some; if it were on all, it would not fall. Their second proof uses an analogy (qiyas): ambiguity in the obliged (ibham al-mukallaf) is like ambiguity in the commanded act (ibham al-fa'l), which is permitted (e.g., choice in kaffarah).
The opposing view is presented as 'qīla' (it is said), indicating it is a minority position.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Responses
The passage provides two refutations. First, the falling of the obligation does not prove it was only on some; the purpose is existence of the act, akin to two guarantors for a debt—whichever pays, the debt falls from both. Second, the analogy to ambiguity in the act is invalid: ambiguity in the act can be resolved by the obliged's choice (e.g., choosing one of three kaffarah options), but ambiguity in the obliged leads to punishing an unspecified person, which is irrational.
The Hanafi response uses a counter-analogy (two guarantors) and exposes a fatal flaw in the opposing qiyas (ma'a al-fariq).
5
Determine the Practical Consequence (Thamarah al-Khilaf)
Consider a scenario where the entire community abandons a fard kifayah (e.g., funeral prayer). According to the Hanafi view, all are sinful because each was obligated. According to the opposing view, only the unspecified part sins—but since that part is unknown, effectively no one can be blamed, making the obligation meaningless. The text clearly favors the Hanafi position.
The practical difference highlights why the Hanafi view is more consistent with the purpose of the Law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة الواجب (الفرض الكفائي وابهام المكلف الواجب على الكفاية واجب على الكل أي كل واحد ويسقط بفعل البعض، ولا يلزم النسخ؛ لأن سقوط الأمر قبل الأداء قد يكون لانتفاء علة الوجوب، وقيل : هو واجب على البعض، لنا إثم الكل بتركه إذا ظنوا أن غيرهم لم يفعل وفي محل وجوب الفرض الكفائي مذهبان : 1 - الأول : أن الوجوب على كل فرد (وحكمه أنه يسقط الواجب بفعل البعض و أدائه، عن الآخرين، فلا يأثمون بتركه، كصلاة الجنازة والجهاد، ولا يلزم أن يكون ترك عمل البعض بذلك الواجب نسخ وجوب العمل عليه قبل العمل به لوجهين : الأول: إنما يكون النسخ بالمنع عن العمل، ولم يمنع الشرع البعض الآخر عن العمل به والثاني : أن سقوط العمل بالأمر قبل الأداء قد يكون لانتفاء علة الوجوب، كسقوط وجوب الزكوة لأجل هلاك النصاب قبل الحول، أو سقوط الصلاة عن الحائض بسبب عدم طهارتها . 2 - والثاني : أنه قيل إن الوجوب أيضاً على البعض المبهم (كما أن الأداء على البعض غير المعين). ودليلنا (دليل الجمهور ) على الوجوب على كل فرد فرد أنهم إذا علموا أن أحداً لم يعمل بالواجب على الكفاية يأثم كلهم، ولو كان الوجوب على البعض لم يأثم كلهم . أدلة القائلين بالوجوب على البعض قالوا :أولاً: سقط بفعل البعض، ولوكان على الكل لم يسقط، قلنا : المقصود وجود الفعل، وقد وجد كسقوط ما على الكفيلين بأداء أحدهما واحتج هؤلاء البعض بثلاثة أدلة : 171 -١ قالوا أولاً : سقوط الواجب بفعل البعض دليل على أن الوجوب أيضاً على البعض، ولو كان الوجوب على كل أحد لم يسقط الواجب بفعل البعض، وهذا هو الفرق بين الواجب العيني والكفائي. قلنا في الجواب عن هذا الدليل : إن المقصود من تشريع ذلك الواجب نفس وجود الفعل المأمور به وقد وجد من البعض فيكفي ذلك في الامتثال) كما إذا كان عن رجل كفيلان فأيهما أدى الدين سقط عن المدين، كذلك هنا أى مكلف أمتثل بالأمر سقط الاثم عن ذمة الباقين . وثانياً : الإبهام في المكلف كالإبهام في المكلف به قلنا تاثيم المبهم غير معقول ٢-(وقالوا) ثانياً : إن الابهام في المكلف المأمور كالابهام في الفعل الذى كلف به، وإذا كان المكلف به (الفعل) المامور به مبهما لا يمكن الامتثال، فكذلك إذا كان المكلف مبهماً. قلنا في الجواب عن دليلهم الثانى : إن قياس ابهام المكلف بابهام المكلف به الفعل الذي كلف به قياس مع الفارق ؛ حيث إن إزالة إبهام المكلف به ممكن، لأن تعيينه عند العمل باختيار المكلف، وهو مخير، كما في كفارة اليمين، فإن الحانث مخير بين أحد الثلاثة : إطعام عشرة مساكين، أو كسوتهم أو تحرير رقبة . ولكن إبهام المكلف غير صحيح، لأنه (في صورة الترك) يلزم تأثيم المبهم، وتأثيم المبهم يستلزم تعذيب غير معين وهو باطل، إذفيه تعذيب غير الآثم.
Issue: The obligatory (fard kifayah) and ambiguity of the obliged. The obligation of sufficiency is obligatory upon all, i.e., upon each one, and it falls by the action of some, and it does not entail abrogation, because the falling of the command before performance may be due to the absence of the cause of obligation. And it is said: it is obligatory upon some. Our proof: the sin of all if they leave it when they think that no one else did it. Regarding the locus of obligation of fard kifayah, there are two schools: First: that the obligation is on every individual, and its ruling is that the obligation falls by the action and performance of some, from the others, so they do not sin by leaving it, like the funeral prayer and jihad. And it is not necessary that the leaving of action by some regarding that obligation be abrogation of the obligation of acting upon it before the action, for two reasons: first: abrogation is only by prohibition of the action, and the Shari'ah did not prohibit the others from acting upon it. Second: the falling of action by command before performance may be due to absence of the cause of obligation, like the falling of obligation of zakat due to destruction of the nisab before the year, or the falling of prayer from the menstruating woman due to lack of purity. Second: that it is said that the obligation is also upon the ambiguous part (just as performance is upon some unspecified). And our proof (the proof of the majority) for obligation on each individual is that if they know that no one acted upon the obligatory of sufficiency, they all sin. And if the obligation were on some, they all would not sin. The proofs of those who say obligation is on some: they said: first: it fell by the action of some; if it were on all, it would not fall. We reply: the purpose is the existence of the act, and it has occurred, like the falling of what is upon two guarantors by the payment of one of them. And these opponents argued with three proofs: 1 - They said first: The falling of the obligation by the action of some is proof that the obligation is also on some; if the obligation were on everyone, the obligation would not fall by the action of some. This is the difference between individual and sufficiency obligation. We reply to this proof: The purpose of legislating that obligation is the very existence of the commanded act, and it has occurred from some, so that suffices for compliance, just as if a man has two guarantors, whichever pays the debt it falls from the debtor. Similarly here, whichever obliged complies with the command, the sin falls from the liability of the rest. Second: The ambiguity in the obliged is like the ambiguity in the obliged act. We reply: Punishing the ambiguous is not rational. 2 - (And they said) secondly: The ambiguity in the commanded obliged is like the ambiguity in the act which is commanded. And if the commanded act (the act) is ambiguous, compliance is impossible, so also if the obliged is ambiguous. We reply to their second proof: The analogy of ambiguity of the obliged with ambiguity of the commanded act is an analogy with a difference, because removing the ambiguity of the commanded act is possible, as its specification at the time of action is by the choice of the obliged, and he has a choice, as in expiation of an oath: the oath-breaker has a choice among three: feeding ten poor, clothing them, or freeing a slave. But ambiguity of the obliged is not valid, because (in case of abandonment) it entails punishing the ambiguous, and punishing the ambiguous necessitates punishing an unspecified person, which is invalid, because it involves punishing the non-sinner.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute regarding the nature of fard kifayah (communal obligation). It presents two madhhabs: the first (majority, which is the Hanafi position) holds that fard kifayah is obligatory on every individual (wajib 'ala kulli wahid), but it falls from all when some perform it. The second holds that the obligation is only on some unspecified individuals. The majority proves its position: if everyone neglects it and knows that no one else did it, they all sin. This shows the obligation is on each. The opponents argue that if it were on all, it would not fall by the act of some. The majority replies: the purpose is the existence of the act, akin to two guarantors where payment by one removes the debt from both. The opponents also argue that ambiguity in the obliged (like fard kifayah where the specific person is not designated) is like ambiguity in the commanded act (like choosing among expiation options). The majority replies: this analogy is invalid because ambiguity in the act can be resolved by the obliged's choice, whereas ambiguity in the obliged leads to punishing an unspecified person if everyone abandons it, which is irrational. Thus, the majority view is preferred.

Key Terms
الفرض الكفائي (al-fard al-kifa'i): An obligation that is required of the community as a whole; if some perform it, the rest are absolved.
الوجوب العيني (al-wujub al-'ayni): Individual obligation that must be performed by each person; cannot be fulfilled by another.
إبهام المكلف (ibham al-mukallaf): Ambiguity in who is obligated; the obligation is on an unspecified person.
القياس مع الفارق (al-qiyas ma'a al-fariq): An analogy that fails because of a significant difference between the original and the new case.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority view presented in the passage, what happens if everyone in the community abandons a fard kifayah?
A) No one is sinful because it is only recommended.
B) Only those who knew about it are sinful.
C) All are sinful because the obligation was on each individual.
D) The obligation is transferred to the next generation.

Key Takeaway
Fard kifayah imposes an obligation on every individual, but the act of some fulfills the duty for all; the Hanafi position is proven by the universal sin in case of neglect and is methodologically superior to the view of an ambiguous obligation.

Rationality of Punishing the Unspecified in Fard Kifaya
Defends rationality of punishing unspecified for fard kifaya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi argument that punishing 'all' for a communal omission is rational because 'all' is an individual of the unspecified 'some'.

Core Idea
Punishing 'all' for a communal omission is rational because 'all' is an individual of the unspecified 'some'.

This passage defends the rationality of punishing an unspecified individual for neglecting a communal duty (fard kifaya). You will follow the Hanafi response to the objection that such punishment is irrational, and understand how the concept of 'all' as an individual of the 'some' resolves the problem. The discussion also invokes Quranic evidence to support the communal nature of the obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection and Hanafi Position
Read the opening objection: 'Punishing the unspecified is irrational.' The Hanafi position holds that sin falls on all due to omission of some. Your first task is to locate the objection and the initial Hanafi response.
The objection is stated in the matn: 'قلنا: تأثيم المبهم غير معقول'.
2
Analyze the First Response
The first Hanafi response argues that omission of some primarily incurs sin on that some, which remains unspecified, leading to the very problem of punishing the unspecified. Understand why this response is insufficient and leads to the second response.
This response is given as: 'قلنا : ترك البعض يقتضي أولاً وبالذات إثم ذلك البعض...'
3
Understand the Second Response: 'All' as an Individual
The second response claims that 'all' (the entire community) is an individual of the unspecified 'some'. If all perform the duty, they fulfill what was obligatory on the some, so punishing all is a form of punishing the some, which is rational. Grasp this key move.
The author states: 'أقول : الكل فرد من البعض المبهم'.
4
Examine the Four Points of Elaboration
The commentator elaborates four points: (1) The unspecified some has two individuals: within the some and within the all. (2) The all's performance proves it is a subset. (3) Punishing the unspecified some is irrational only if it does not include the all. (4) The word 'reflect' (تفكر) indicates the original problem lingers for the essential sin. Parse these points to solidify the reasoning.
These points follow 'ومحصل هذا القول أمور'.
5
Consider the Quranic Proof
The passage cites Quran 9:122 as evidence for the communal obligation. The Hanafi interpretation reconciles it with the fard kifaya framework by treating it as fulfilled by the action of some. Note how the verse is used to support the position.
The verse: 'فَلَوْلَا نَفَرَ مِن كُلِّ فِرْقَةٍ مِّنْهُمْ طَائِفَةٌ'.

والثالث: أنّ البعض المبهم غير الشامل لفرده الآخر، وهو الكل غير معقول تأثيمه، لاستلزامه تعذيب المبهم ، وأما في صورة شمول البعض المبهم الكل فلا يلزم تأثيب المبهم، فهو معقول .

And this is an explanation of the author's statement: 'We say: punishing the unspecified is irrational.' It was said: Rather, their school is the sin of all due to the omission of some. We say: The omission of some necessitates primarily and essentially the sin of that some, although it leads to the sin of all secondarily and accidentally, so punishing the unspecified is necessary. I say: The 'all' is an individual of the unspecified 'some', for if all perform it, they have performed what was obligatory upon them by agreement, so the sin of all is an individual of the sin of some, and this kind of punishing the unspecified is completely rational, because it does not negate attainment. Yes, the unspecified that does not encompass the all, i.e., in terms of being unspecified, is irrational, so reflect. And when the objector refuted this second answer, the author transmitted that refutation by his saying: 'It was said: their school (the school of the proponents of the second proof that the sin of all, all the obligated, occurs) due to the omission of some (the obligatory).' Then he answered that refutation by saying: 'We said...' The summary of the refutation or answer from the side of the proponents of the second proof against the author's answer regarding the necessity of punishing the unspecified, which is irrational as follows: This (punishing the unspecified or punishing the unspecified person) is necessary only if we say the sin is on some, but we do not say that; rather, we say the sin is on all due to the omission of unspecified some. Then the author said: We say the omission of what is obligatory as a communal duty necessitates the sin of that some primarily and essentially, although the omission leads and returns to the sin of all, all individuals of the obligated, secondarily and accidentally. Thus the omission of some and its sin leads to the sin of all, so the some that sinned primarily and essentially remains unspecified, so punishing the unspecified is necessary, and punishing the all does not help. And after the first answer (about the sin of all due to omission of some), the author began the second answer about it and said: I say: The all, all individuals of the obligated, are individuals of the unspecified some, for if all perform it (the communal duty), they have performed in reality what was obligatory upon them by agreement, so the sin of all is an individual of the sin of some, and this kind of punishing the unspecified is completely rational, because it does not negate the attainment (attainment of the unspecified obligatory some). Yes, the unspecified that does not encompass the all, i.e., in terms of being unspecified, is irrational. The summary of this statement comprises matters: First: That the unspecified some upon which the obligation lies, and in the case of omission sin is upon it, has two individuals: one is realized within that some, some individuals, and the other is realized within the all, i.e., all individuals. So the first individual of that some is unspecified and [unclear] so its punishment in the case of omission is irrational, and the second individual of it (which is the all) does not negate the attainment of sin due to omission, and punishing the second individual (the all) for omission of the unspecified some is rational, so that punishing the unspecified and punishing the specified person is not necessary. Second: That his saying: 'For if all perform it...' is evidence for his saying: 'The all is from the individuals of the unspecified some.' Its summary is that if all the obligated perform the communal duty, then it is a performance of what was obligatory upon them by agreement, and the obligation was upon the unspecified some, and the performance is realized by the action of all, so it is known that the performance of the all of the obligation is an individual of the performance of the some, just as the sin of the some falls by its falling from the all, so it is established by correspondence that the sin of the all is an individual of the sin of the unspecified some. Third: That the unspecified some that does not include its other individual, which is the all, its punishment is irrational, because it necessitates punishing the unspecified, but in the case of the unspecified some including the all, it does not necessitate punishing the unspecified, so it is rational. Fourth: That in his saying 'reflect' is an indication of the persistence of the original problem, which is punishing the some that matters essentially, as for punishing the all, it is secondary and accidental, so it is not considered. And third: Allah said: 'If only a group from every faction...' We said: The mentioned (verse) is interpreted as being fulfilled by the action of some, to combine the evidences. And they (those who say obligation is on some and sin on some in case of omission) said thirdly (in their argument): Allah said: 'If only from every faction among them a group would go forth to gain understanding in religion.' The detail of the argument: seeking knowledge is from the communal duties, and 'faction' is a large group.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a statement from the matn arguing that it is irrational to punish an unspecified individual (mubham) for neglecting a communal obligation (fard kifaya). The commentator defends this view against objections. The first objection claims that the sin is on all, not on an unspecified some. The author responds that the omission of some initially incurs sin on that some, which is unspecified, leading to the irrationality of punishing the unspecified. He then offers a second response: the 'all' is actually an individual of the unspecified 'some'—since if all perform the duty, they fulfill the obligation that was on the unspecified some. Therefore, punishing the all is a form of punishing the some, and this is rational because it achieves the purpose of securing the obligation. The commentator elaborates four points: (1) The unspecified some has two individuals—one within the some and one within the all; punishing the all (the second individual) is rational. (2) The fact that all performing the duty fulfills the obligation proves that the all is a subset of the some. (3) If the unspecified some does not include the all, punishing it is irrational, but if it does, it is rational. (4) The word 'reflect' hints that the original problem remains—the essential sin is on the some. Then a third argument from the Quran (9:122) is introduced: the verse commands some to go for knowledge, which is a communal duty; the author interprets it as fulfilled by the action of some, reconciling evidences.

Key Terms
تأثيم المبهم (ta'thim al-mubham): Punishing or holding sinful an unspecified individual (not determined) for neglect of a duty
فرد كفاية (fard kifaya): A communal obligation that is fulfilled if some perform it, removing sin from all
مبهم (mubham): Unspecified, undetermined individual or set of individuals
الكل (al-kull): All individuals of the obligated community
البعض (al-ba'd): Some, a part; the unspecified group initially obligated in fard kifaya

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second response in the passage, why is punishing the 'all' for omission of a communal duty rational?
A) Because the 'all' are sinning by not performing the duty individually.
B) Because the 'all' is an individual of the unspecified 'some', and punishment is a form of punishing that 'some'.
C) Because the Quran directly commands punishment of all for communal neglect.
D) Because the unspecified individual cannot be found, so all must be punished as a deterrent.

Key Takeaway
Punishing all for neglect of a fard kifaya is rational because the all is a subset of the unspecified some upon whom the obligation originally rests.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
10 min
Nature of Obligation in Wajib Mukhayyar
Analyzing whether the required act is one unspecified or the entire set

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the nature of obligation in wajib mukhayyar and evaluate the opposing argument using textual and rational proofs.

Core Idea
In wajib mukhayyar, the obligation is a single unspecified act, not the entire set — the particle 'aw' proves choice.

This lesson examines a key usul al-fiqh dispute: when the Lawgiver gives multiple options to fulfill an obligation (e.g., kaffara options of feeding, clothing, or freeing a slave), is the wajib one of those options, or is it the entire set discharged by performing any one? The Hanafi school, represented by the 'fourth school' in this text, argues that it is one unspecified act, using both rational and textual proofs. We will analyze the arguments and the author's response to the opposing view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
What exactly is being debated? The nature of the obligatory act in a wajib mukhayyar (an obligation with multiple alternatives): is the wajib a single unspecified act (badal) or the entire collective (majmoo')?
2
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi position (the fourth school) holds that the wajib is one unspecified act determined in Allah's knowledge. The obligation is discharged by performing any one of the stipulated alternatives.
This is known as 'al-wajib al-mukhayyar' — the obligatory act is one among options.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proofs
Two proofs are offered: rational (it is possible for the Lawgiver to command one of several acts) and textual (the Quranic particle 'aw' indicates choice, not conjunction). Focus on the textual proof: the use of 'aw' in the kaffara verse shows that the obligation is one of the three options.
The rational proof: the universal concept of 'one of these' exists in each individual act.
4
Examine the opposing argument
Opponents argue that obligating an unspecified act is impossible because the act is unknown and obligation requires specification and knowledge.
They claim the wajib must be the entire collective discharged by a part.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The author responds: the unspecified act is known in two ways — (1) by its attribute of obligatoriness, and (2) by being one of the known three options. Additionally, performing any one alternative actualizes the unspecified act. The impossibility only arises if the act were to be performed while remaining unspecified externally, which is not the case.
This shows that knowledge of the unknown can be relational and sufficient for obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المذهب الرابع: أنه قيل إن الأمر الواجب من الأمور المتعددة في الواجب المخير متعين عند الله لا يختلف باختلاف المكلفين لكن يسقط الواجب بأداء ذلك الأمر المعين، وبأمر آخر من تلك الأمور المتعددة. الدليل على أن الواجب في الواجب المخير أمر واحد، دون الجميع. والدليل لنا على أن الواجب في الواجب المخير هو أمر واحد من الأمور المتعددة على سبيل البدلية، لا المجموع المؤدى بالبعض أمران: عقلي ونقلي. أما الأول: فالجواز عقلاً، لأن الشارع كأنه قال: أوجبت عليكم أحد هذه الأمور من الإطعام والكسوة والتحرير وهذا الإيجاب جائز عقلاً، لأن مصداق أحد هذه الأمور موجود في الخارج في ضمن أفراد تلك الأمور، والمفهوم الكلي المتردد بين الأفراد الثلاثة المأخوذ من الأمور المعلومة أيضاً موجود في الخارج في ضمن أفراده، فجاز طلبه. وأما الثاني: فإن النص دل عليه أيضاً، فإن الأمور الثلاثة ذكرت بكلمة (أو) وهي تأتي لأحد الأمور أو أحد الأمرين، ولو كان المطلوب المجموع لجاءت كلمة (واو) دون (أو)، فالنص دل بظاهره على وقوع ما جوزه العقل. أدلة من قال: إن الواجب في الواجب المخير هو الجميع المؤدى بالبعض. قالوا في نفي التخيير أولاً: غير المعين مجهول، ويستحيل وقوعه فلا يكلف به. قلنا: إنه معلوم من حيث أنه واجب، وهو مفهوم الواحد من الثلاثة، ويقع ذلك الواحد بوقوع كل، وإنما يستحيل لو كلف بإيقاعه غير معين في الخارج. وذكر المصنف ثلاثة أدلة من جانب أصحاب المذهب الثاني: أ - قالوا: القائلون بإيجاب الجميع ونفي التخيير: الواحد المبهم غير المعين الذي يأتي به المكلف في الواجب المخير هو مجهول، والمجهول لا يكلف به أحد، فالأمر الواحد المبهم لا يكلف به أحد، لأن التكليف بشيء فرع تعيينه وعلم به، وإنما يكون التعيين بعد وجوده وكذا علمه أيضاً يحصل بعد وجوده، ويستحيل وقوعه قبل التعيين والعلم، فلا يكلف بالمستحيل أحد. وأجاب المصنف عن دليلهم هذا بقوله: قلنا: لا نسلم أنه مجهول، بل إنه معلوم للمكلف من وجهين: 1- من حيث أنه واجب (أي الأمر الواحد المبهم موصوف بالواجبية). 2- ومن حيث هو مفهوم الواحد من الثلاثة (أي) كونه متصفاً بأنه واحد من الثلاثة المعلومة في الآية. وهذا النوع من العلم كافٍ في إمكان وقوعه والإتيان به. وأشار المصنف إلى الوجه الثالث من العلم بقوله: ويقع بوقوع كل، أي يمكن وجود ذلك الواحد والإتيان به في ضمن الكل، فإنه لو جاء المكلف بكل الثلاثة يأتي بواحد منها أيضاً، فإن وجود الكل يستلزم وجود الجزء. وإنما يستلزم الاستحالة التي ذكرها المستدل لو كلف الفاعل بإيقاع ذلك الواحد والإتيان به حال كونه غير معين في الخارج، وليس كذلك، بل يعلمه المكلف بثلاثة أنواع من العلم، وهو موجود في الخارج. [مارها واموات؟] وثانياً: كون الواجب أحدهما، والتخيير فيه متناقض. قلنا: الواجب المبهم والمخير فيه المتعينات وذلك جائز كوجوب أحد النقيضين مع إمكان كل منهما. 2- و (قالوا) ثانياً (في دليل إيجاب الجميع ونفي التخيير) أن كون الواجب أحدهما (الواحد المبهم أو الجميع) والتخيير فيه متناقضان، يعني في وجوب إبهام أحد الأمور الثلاثة، وفي وجوب الجميع الشامل لذلك الواحد تناقض.
The fourth school: It is said that the obligatory matter among multiple matters in the optional obligatory (wajib mukhayyar) is determined in the sight of Allah, not differing according to the obligated individuals, but the obligation is discharged by performing that determined matter, or by another matter among those multiple matters. The proof that the obligatory in the optional obligatory is one matter, not the entirety. The proof for us that the obligatory in the optional obligatory is one matter from among multiple matters on the basis of substitution, not the collective discharged by a part, are two: rational and textual. As for the first: it is rationally permissible, because the Lawgiver as if said: 'I have obligated upon you one of these matters: feeding, clothing, or freeing [a slave]' and this obligation is rationally permissible, because the instantiation of one of these matters exists externally within the individuals of those matters, and the universal concept fluctuating among the three known individuals also exists externally within its individuals, so requesting it is permissible. As for the second: the text indicates it as well, for the three matters are mentioned with the word (aw) which is used for one of the matters or one of the two things; if the required were the collective, the word (waw) would have come instead of (aw). So the text by its apparent meaning indicates the occurrence of what reason deemed permissible. The proofs of those who say that the obligatory in the optional obligatory is the entire collective discharged by a part. They said in negating choice first: the unspecified non-determined is unknown, and its occurrence is impossible, so one cannot be obligated with it. We said: It is known in that it is obligatory, and it is the concept of the one from among the three, and that one occurs by the occurrence of each, and it would only be impossible if one were obligated to bring it into existence while it is unspecified externally. The author mentioned three proofs from the side of the proponents of the second school: 1- They said (the proponents of obligating the entirety and negating choice): the unspecified single non-determined that the obligated person performs in the optional obligatory is unknown, and the unknown cannot be obligated upon anyone, so that single unspecified matter is not obligated upon anyone, because obligation of something is conditional upon its specification and knowledge thereof, and specification only occurs after its existence, and likewise its knowledge is only obtained after its existence, and its occurrence before specification and knowledge is impossible, so one cannot be obligated with the impossible. The author responded to their proof by saying: We do not accept that it is unknown; rather it is known to the obligated person from two aspects: 1- In that it is obligatory (i.e., that single unspecified matter is qualified by obligatoriness). 2- And in that it is the concept of the one from among the three (i.e., it is qualified as being one of the three known in the verse). And this type of knowledge is sufficient for the possibility of its occurrence and performing it. The author indicated the third aspect of knowledge by his saying: And it occurs by the occurrence of each, meaning that existence of that one and performing it can be within the entirety, for if the obligated person brings all three, he also brings one of them, because the existence of the whole entails the existence of the part. It would only entail the impossibility mentioned by the objector if the doer were obligated to bring that one while it is unspecified externally, but this is not the case; rather the obligated person knows it by three types of knowledge, and it exists externally. [؟]. And secondly: that the obligatory is one of them, and choice in it is contradictory. We said: The unspecified obligatory and the choice among determined things is permissible, like the obligation of one of two opposites with the possibility of each. 2- And they said secondly (in proof of obligating the entirety and negating choice) that the obligatory being one of them (the single unspecified or the entirety) and choice in it are contradictory, meaning in obligating the ambiguity of one of the three matters, and in obligating the entirety inclusive of that one, there is contradiction.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage presents a theological-legal dispute about the nature of the obligation in a wajib mukhayyar (optional obligatory act, e.g., the kaffara of fasting or feeding or freeing in expiation). The fourth school (the position defended by the author) holds that the wajib is a single unspecified act determined in Allah's knowledge, but the obligation is discharged by performing any one of the specified alternatives. Two proofs are given: rational (it is possible for the Lawgiver to command one of several actions) and textual (the use of the particle 'aw' in the verse indicates choice, as opposed to 'waw' which would indicate conjunction). The opposing view argues that requiring an unspecified act is impossible because it is unknown and unknowable; the obligation must be on the entire set, discharged by performing any one part. The author responds that the unspecified act is known in two ways: by its attribute of obligatoriness, and by being one of the known three; moreover, it exists within the performance of any one alternative. The second objection about contradiction is dismissed by analogy with the obligation of one of two opposites (e.g., belief or disbelief is not commanded, but the principle shows the possibility of choice). The passage is a classic usul dispute relying on textual analysis and rational argumentation.

Key Terms
الواجب المخير (al-wājib al-mukhayyar): An obligatory act where the Lawgiver gives a choice among several alternative actions, any one of which fulfills the obligation.
البدلية (al-badalīyah): Substitution; the obligation is attached to a single unspecified act that is substituted by any one of the alternatives.
المجموع المؤدى بالبعض (al-majmūʿ al-muʾaddā bil-baʿḍ): The collective entity that is discharged by performing a part; the opposing view that the wajib is the entire set of options.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fourth school (the author's position), what is the function of the particle 'أو' (aw) in the verse on expiation?
A) It indicates that all three options are obligatory together.
B) It indicates that one of the three options is obligatory, showing choice.
C) It indicates that the obligation is ambiguous and cannot be known.
D) It indicates that the obligation is the entire collective discharged by a part.

Key Takeaway
In wajib mukhayyar, the obligation is a single unspecified act (badal) determined by Allah, and the particle 'aw' in the Quran indicates choice, not conjunction.

The Knownness of the Ambiguous Act in Mukhayyar Obligation
Opponent's proof and Hanafi rebuttal on the knownness of the ambiguous act

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the opponent's argument against mukhayyar obligation and explain the Hanafi threefold response.

Core Idea
The ambiguous act is known through its attributes and possibility, so obligation is valid.

This passage addresses a fundamental dispute in usul al-fiqh: can the Lawgiver obligate an act that is not specifically determined until performed? The opponent argues it is impossible because the act is unknown. The Hanafi school, however, demonstrates that the act is known in three respects, making the obligation valid. Understanding this argument builds your ability to defend Hanafi methodology in cases of choice-based obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
Locate the statement that the ambiguous one act is unknown and cannot be obligated.
The opponent says 'al-wahid al-mubham… huwa majhul'.
2
Extract the opponent's reasoning
Understand why they think the act is unknown: obligation requires determination and knowledge, which only occur after existence.
Their proof: 'al-taklif bi-shay'in far'un 'ala al-ta'ayyun wa al-'ilm bihi'.
3
State the Hanafi counter-claim
The author says 'la nusallimu annahu majhul' – we do not concede it is unknown.
4
List the three respects of knowledge
Identify the three ways the act is known: (a) as wajib, (b) as one of the three known options, (c) as realizable by performing all (part-whole).
The text says 'ma'lum… min wajhayn' then adds a third 'wa yaqa'u bi-wuqu'i kull'.
5
Explain why the opponent's impossibility does not apply
The impossibility only arises if the act were obligated as undetermined externally; here it is known sufficiently, so obligation is valid.
The author says 'innama yastalzimu al-istihalata… law kullifa… bi-wuqu'i dhālika al-wāḥid… ḥāla kawnihi ghayra mu'ayyanin fī al-khārij'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر المصنف ثلاثة أدلة من جانب أصحاب المذهب الثاني: -١ - قالوا ( القائلون بإيجاب الجميع ونفي التخيير بين الجميع والبعض أولاً : الواحد المبهم غير المعيّن الذي يأتي به المكلّف الواجب المخيّر هو مجهول، والمجهول لا يكلف به أحد، فأحد الأمور المعلومة لا يكلف به أحد، لأن التكليف بشيئي فرع التعين وعلم به، وإنما يكون التعين بعد وجوده وكذا علمه أيضاً يحصل بعد وجوده ، ويستحيل وقوعه ( وجوده ) قبل التعيين والعلم، فلا يكلف بالمستحيل أحد وأجاب المصنف عن دليلهم هذا بقوله: قلنا : ( لا نسلم أنه مجهول، بل إنه معلوم المكلّف من وجهين : ١ من حيث أنه واجب (أي الأمر الواحد المبهم موصوف بالواجبية). ٢ ومن حيث هو مفهوم الواحد من الثلاثة أي) كونه متصفاً بأنه واحد من الثلاثة المعلومة في الآية) وهذا النوع من العلم كافٍ في إمكان وقوعه والاتيان به. وأشار المصنف إلى الوجه الثالث من العلم بقوله : و يقع بوقوع كلّ أي يمكن وجود ذلك الواحد واتيان المكلف به في ضمن الكلّ، فإنه لو جاء المكلف بكل الثلاثة يأتي بواحد منها أيضاً، فانّ وجود الكل يستلزم وجود الجزء . وإنما يستلزم الاستحالة التي ذكرها المستدل لو كلّف الفاعل بايقاع ذلك الواحد والاتيان به حال كونه غير معين في الخارج، وليس كذلك، بل يعلمه المكلّف بثلاثة أنواع من العلم، وهو موجود في الخارج . [؟]
The author mentioned three proofs from the side of the adherents of the second school: 1) They said (those who assert the obligation of all and deny choice between all and some first): The one ambiguous undetermined act that the obligated person performs in a mukhayyar obligation is unknown, and the unknown cannot be obligated upon anyone. So one of the known matters cannot be obligated upon anyone, because obligation of a thing is dependent on its determination and knowledge thereof. Determination only occurs after its existence, and likewise its knowledge is only attained after its existence. It is impossible for it to occur (exist) before determination and knowledge, so the impossible cannot be obligated upon anyone. The author responded to this proof of theirs by saying: We say: We do not concede that it is unknown; rather, it is known to the obligated person in two respects: 1) In that it is wajib (i.e., the one ambiguous command is described with obligation). 2) In that it is the concept of 'one of the three' (i.e., it is qualified as being one of the three known in the verse). This type of knowledge is sufficient for the possibility of its occurrence and performance. The author pointed to the third respect of knowledge by saying: And it occurs by the occurrence of all, i.e., that one can exist and the obligated person can perform it within the entirety, for if the obligated person performs all three, he performs one of them as well, since the existence of the whole necessitates the existence of the part. The impossibility mentioned by the arguer would only follow if the doer were obligated to bring about that one and perform it while it is undetermined externally; but it is not so. Rather, the obligated person knows it through three types of knowledge, and it exists externally. [؟]
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute in usul al-fiqh regarding the nature of a mukhayyar (optional) obligation. The opposing school argues that the ambiguous single act (al-wahid al-mubham) cannot be the object of obligation because it is unknown until performed. The Hanafi position, as defended by the commentator, is that the act is known in three ways: (1) it is known as wajib, (2) it is known conceptually as one of the three specified acts, and (3) it can be realized by performing all three (since the whole includes the part). The commentator refutes the opponent's claim of impossibility, showing that the act is knowable and thus obligation is valid.

Key Terms
واجب مخيّر (wajib mukhayyar): An obligation where the obligated person may choose among several specified acts.
مبهم (mubham): Ambiguous; not specifically determined among the options.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
The ambiguous act in a mukhayyar obligation is known through its wajib nature, its membership in a set, and its realisability via the whole, making obligation valid.

The Hanafi Response to the Alleged Contradiction in Ambiguous Obligation and Choice
Refutation of the claim that ambiguous obligation and choice are contradictory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction between the ambiguous obligation (wājib mubham) and specific choice (takhayyur) to refute the claim of contradiction, and analogize it to the obligation of one of two opposites.

Core Idea
No contradiction: the obligation is on the ambiguous kind, the choice is on the specific instance.

The opponent argues that requiring one unspecified act while allowing choice among specific acts is contradictory. The Hanafi school responds by distinguishing two levels: the obligation attaches to the ambiguous category (the overall duty), while the choice (takhayyur) operates among the specific alternatives that fulfill it. This is not a contradiction but a coherent structure, analogous to the rational obligation of one of two logical opposites—such as either the existence or non-existence of Zaid—which is perfectly possible.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the Opposing Claim
Locate the opponent's argument: they assert that making the obligation ambiguous (one unspecified act) while allowing choice among specific acts is contradictory. The contradiction is supposed to arise because the obligation cannot be both ambiguous and specific simultaneously.
The opponent's claim is presented as 'wa qālū thāniyan' in the text.
2
2. Extract the Hanafi Distinction
The Hanafi response states: 'al-wājibu huwa al-wāhidu al-mubham, wa al-mukhayyaru fīhi hiya al-umūru al-thalāthatu al-mutāyyanāt' – the obligated act is the single ambiguous one, while the choice is among the three specific acts. These operate on different levels, so no contradiction.
Note the key terms: wājib mubham (ambiguous obligation) vs. mukhayyar fīhi (the object of choice).
3
3. Analyze the Analogy (Qiyas)
The text analogizes this to the obligation of one of two logical opposites (wujūb aḥad al-naqīḍayn) – e.g., requiring either Zaid's existence or non-existence. This is possible because the obligation attaches to the disjunction, not to each specific. Similarly, the ambiguous obligation demands that one of the three specific acts be performed, but not all.
The logical opposites are existence and non-existence, which are mutually exclusive but can be part of a conditional obligation.
4
4. Application: Understand the Thamara (Practical Consequence)
In practice, the Hanafi view allows the obligated person to fulfill the duty by performing any one of the specific options (e.g., in kaffārah, choosing one of the three expiations). The opposing view that sees the obligation as covering all options would require performing all three.
This difference affects actual legal practice, especially in matters of kaffārah or other mukhayyar obligations.
5
5. Critically Evaluate the Analogy
Consider whether the analogy holds: the obligation of one of two opposites is a purely rational necessity to avoid contradicting the law of non-contradiction, while the ambiguous obligation in sharī‘a is a specific legal structure. The Hanafi position is that the principle of possibility (jawāz) applies in both cases.
This step reinforces the strength of the aqli (rational) dalīl.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانياً: كون الواجب أحدهما، والتخيير فيه متناقض قلنا: الواجب المبهم والمخير فيه المتعينات وذلك جائز كوجوب أحد النقيضين مع إمكان كل منهما ٢- و (قالوا) ثانياً ( في دليل إيجاب الجميع ونفي التخيير ) أن كون الواجب أحدهما الواحد المبهم أو الجميع) والتخيير فيه متناقضان، يعني في وجوب إبهام أحد الأمور الثلاثة، وفي وجوب الجميع الشامل لذلك الواحد تناقض. ثم أجاب عنه بقوله : قلنا : الواجب هو الواحد المبهم، والمخير فيه هى الأمور الثلاثة المتعينات، وذلك جائز ، كوجوب أحد النقيضين مع إمكان كل منهما ، كما أنه لا بد من وجود زيد فقط أو عدمه فقط، حتى لا يلزم اجتماع النقيضين في جمعهما ولا ارتفاع النقيضين في رفعهما، مع أن وجود أحدهما وحده، أو عدمه وحده ممكن، فكذلك الواحد المبهم وحده أو الكل (الجميع) هنا ممكن .
And secondly: That the obligation is one of them, and the choice within it is contradictory. We said: The obligation is ambiguous, and what is chosen are the specific matters; that is permissible, like the obligation of one of two opposites with the possibility of each. 2) And they said secondly (as a proof for the obligation of all and denial of choice): That the obligation being one of them (the one ambiguous or all) and the choice within it are contradictory, meaning that the obligation of ambiguity of one of the three matters, and the obligation of the entirety that includes that one, is contradictory. Then he responded by saying: We said: The obligated is the one ambiguous, and the choice is among the three specific matters, and that is permissible, like the obligation of one of two opposites with the possibility of each. Just as it is necessary that Zaid exist only or not exist only, so that neither the conjunction of opposites would occur nor the removal of opposites, while the existence of one alone or its non-existence alone is possible; similarly, the one ambiguous alone or the whole (all) here is possible.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage addresses a second argument from the opposing school: they claim that making the obligation ambiguous (i.e., one unspecified act) while allowing choice among specific acts is contradictory. The Hanafi response clarifies that the obligation is on the single ambiguous act (which is known conceptually), while the choice (takhayyur) is among the three specific acts (al-mutayyanat). There is no contradiction because the ambiguous act is a conceptual category, and the choice specifies which actual act fulfills it. The author analogizes to the obligation of one of two logical opposites (such as existence or non-existence of Zaid) which is possible. Just as the law can require that either Zaid exist or not exist (without specifying which), similarly the mukhayyar obligation is possible.

Key Terms
تخيير (takhayyur): The permissibility or requirement to choose among specified alternatives in fulfilling an obligation.
نقيضين (naqidayn): Two logical opposites (existence and non-existence) that cannot both be true or false simultaneously.
مبهم (mubham): Ambiguous or unspecified; in usul, an obligation that is not tied to a specific act but to a category.
متعينات (mutāyyanāt): Specific, determinate acts; the objects that are specified for choice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Obligation of fasting and charity
D) Obligation of all three kaffarah options

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school establishes that an ambiguous obligation with choice is not contradictory by distinguishing the level of the obligation (ambiguous category) from the level of choice (specific instance), supported by the analogy to the rational possibility of obliging one of two opposites.

Refuting the Analogy of Mukhayyar to Kifayah Obligation
Refutation of an opponent's qiyas linking mukhayyar and kifayah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the flaw in analogizing mukhayyar to kifayah obligations and apply the Hanafi distinction to reject the analogy.

Core Idea
Punishing an unspecified person is irrational; a known person can be punished for leaving an unspecified act.

This lesson examines the third argument of those who claim that all options in a mukhayyar obligation are obligatory. They analogize it to a communal (kifayah) obligation, arguing that both involve an ambiguous fulfillment. The Hanafi response reveals a critical flaw: the rationality of punishment differs between the two cases, breaking the analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's qiyas
State the asl (kifayah) and the far' (mukhayyar). Identify the claimed common illa: attainment of benefit through an ambiguous act or person.
The opponent argues that just as in kifayah the obligation falls on all but performance by some suffices, in mukhayyar all options are obligatory but one suffices.
2
Locate the Hanafi refutation
Find where the author says 'قلنا'. The refutation focuses on the rationality of punishment (ta'thim).
3
Analyze the difference in punishability
In kifayah, if no one performs, punishment would apply to unspecified individuals, which is irrational. In mukhayyar, the obligated person is known, so punishment for leaving an unspecified option is rational.
This difference in the subject of punishment invalidates the analogy.
4
Conclude the invalidity of the analogy
Since the analogy fails, the conclusion that all options are obligatory is incorrect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و (قالوا) ثالثاً (في دليل إيجاب الجميع ونفي التخيير) : الوجوب بالجميع في (الوجوب) المخيّر كالوجوب على الجميع (في الواجب على سبيل) الكفاية، فإن المقتضى فيهما واحد، وهو حصول المصلحة بمبهم . والحاصل أنّ في الواجب الكفالى كما يكون الوجوب على الجميع (جميع المكلّفين) ويتحقق الأداء بفعل البعض، كذلك في الواجب المخير يكون الواجب جميع الأمور المعلومة ويحصل تفريغ ذمة المكلف بأداء بعض منها، فإن الغرض المصلحة من الواجب الكفائي هو وجود الفعل المأمور به، ومن الواجب المخير تفريغ المكلف ذمته عن أداء الواجب المتعدد مصداقه وهما يحصلان من المكلف المبهم والفعل المبهم . وأجاب المصنف عن هذا الدليل بقوله : قلنا : تأثيم فرد واحد غير معين (في الوجوب على البعض دون الكل ( في الواجب الكفائي ) غير معقول، لان تعذيب غير المعين غير معقول وعجز عن معرفة المعين. وأما التأثيم بترك فعل واحد مبهم من الأفعال المعلومة فمعقول ؛ لأن التارك ،معلوم، فقياس الواجب المخير على الواجب الكفالى قياس مع الفارق وغير صحيح، فكذلك القول بوجوب الجميع غير صحيح .
And they said thirdly (as a proof for the obligation of all and denial of choice): The obligation upon all in the mukhayyar obligation is like the obligation upon all (in the communal obligation, i.e., kifayah), for the motive in both is one, which is the attainment of benefit through an ambiguous act. The point is that in the communal obligation, just as the obligation is upon all (all obligated persons) and performance is realized by the act of some, similarly in the mukhayyar obligation, the obligation is upon all the known matters and the discharge of the obligated person's liability is obtained by performing some of them. For the purpose of benefit from the communal obligation is the existence of the commanded act, and from the mukhayyar obligation is the discharge of the obligated person's liability from performing the obligation having multiple realizations; and both are achieved by the ambiguous obligated person and the ambiguous act. The author responded to this proof by saying: We say: Punishment of one unspecified individual (in the obligation upon some and not all in the communal obligation) is irrational, because punishment of an unspecified person is irrational and inability to know the specified one. As for punishment for leaving one ambiguous act among known acts, it is rational because the abandoner is known. So analogizing the mukhayyar obligation to the communal obligation is an analogy with a difference and is incorrect. Thus, the statement of obligation of all is also incorrect.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument of the opposing school: they analogize a mukhayyar (optional) obligation to a kifayah (communal) obligation. In communal duty, all are addressed but performance by some suffices; similarly, they argue, in a mukhayyar obligation, all options are obligatory but performing any one discharges the duty. The Hanafi response refutes this analogy by highlighting a crucial difference: in communal duty, if no one performs, punishment would apply to unspecified individuals, which is irrational because the specific sinner cannot be identified. In contrast, in a mukhayyar obligation, if the obligated person fails to perform any option, punishment is rational because the specific person (the obligated individual) is known. Therefore, the analogy is invalid, and the Hanafi position that only one option is obligatory stands.

Key Terms
واجب كفائي (wajib kifa'i): A collective obligation upon all, fulfilled if some perform it.
تأثيم (ta'thim): Punishment for sin; the act of declaring someone sinful.
مخير (mukhayyar): An obligation with multiple options, any one of which fulfills the duty.
قياس مع الفارق (qiyas ma'a al-fariq): An analogy that includes a material difference, thus invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi response reject the analogy between mukhayyar and kifayah obligations?
A) Because the benefit is different in each
B) Because punishing an unspecified individual in kifayah is irrational, while punishing a known individual for leaving an unspecified act is rational
C) Because the ambiguous act is unknown
D) Because both obligations require all options

Key Takeaway
A valid qiyas requires that the illa be truly common; a difference in the rationality of punishment invalidates the analogy between mukhayyar and kifayah.
