The Substitute (Badal) and the Unity of Obligation When All Alternatives Are Performed
Analyzing the three possibilities when performing all wajib alternatives together

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi concept of badal (substitute) to determine the ruling when a mukallaf performs all specified alternatives of a wajib, and critically evaluate the Minhaj view on multiple complete causes.

Core Idea
Performing all alternatives does not make each obligatory; the whole is a substitute for one unspecified wajib.

When a wajib comes with multiple alternatives, such as the three options for kaffarah, a practical question arises: what if someone performs all alternatives together? Does the obligation attach to all, to each, or to a single one? This lesson unpacks a nuanced Hanafi usul debate. The author, Badakhshani, refines the concept of badal (substitute) to show that the whole set functions as a substitute for an unspecified single alternative, so only one specific alternative actually fulfills the wajib. He then critiques the view from al-Minhaj that each alternative is an independent valid compliance.

Guided Path
1
State the three possibilities
Identify the three logical possibilities when all alternatives are performed: compliance by all (making all wajib), compliance by each (multiple obligations), or compliance by one unspecified (which must then become specific).
These are the first sentences of the passage.
2
Identify the Hanafi position and its proof
The author holds that only one specific alternative is obligatory. His proof uses an analogy: just as the absence of two parts is a complete cause for the absence of the whole (substituting for the absence of one part), so performing all alternatives is a substitute for performing one unspecified alternative.
The key analogy: عدم الجزءين علة تامة لعدم الكل.
3
Identify the opposing view (Minhaj)
The Minhaj view argues that compliance with each alternative is valid because these alternatives are 'definers' (mu'arrafat) and not real causes, so multiple complete causes can coexist without conflict.
This view would allow all three to be independently valid.
4
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The author rebuts that each alternative is an effective cause on a substitutional basis (على سبيل البدلية), making them no different from real causes. Therefore, multiple independent causes cannot converge on a single effect (compliance).
Thus the Minhaj view is rejected as 'apparently objectionable' (وفيه نظر ظاهر).
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
In practice, if a person performs all three kaffarah alternatives simultaneously, the Hanafi view holds that only one specific alternative fulfills the obligation (the one that emerges as mu'ayyan). The Minhaj view would consider all three as valid acts of compliance, potentially creating multiple obligations.
The dispute affects whether intention needs to be specified and whether the act is considered fully discharged.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثلاثة : وثانياً لو أتى بالكل معاً، فالامتثال إما بالكل، فيجب الكل، أو بكل واحد فيلزم تعدد العلل التامة أو بواحد لا بعينه وهو غير موجود فتعين المعين
أقول: لا يلزم وجوب الكل بالامتثال بالكل ، وإنما يلزم لولم يكن بدلاً، ألا ترى أن عدم الجزء علة تامة لعدم الكل، فإذا عدم الجزآن كان المجموع هو العلة التامة
وردّ عليه المصنف فقال: أقول (أولا) باختيار الشق الأول إنه لا يلزم وجوب الكل لأجل الامتثال بالكل، لكون الكل فرداً من مفهوم أحد الثلاثة، ووجوده في ضمن الثلاثة، وإنما يلزم ما قلتم من وجوب الكل بالامتثال به لولم يكن الكل بدلاً عن واحد غير معين وهنا الكل بدل عن واحد غير معين.
ثم ذكر الدليل التنويرى وقال : ألا ترى أن عدم الجزء علة تامة لعدم الكل، فإذا عدم الجزآن كان المجموع هو العلة التامة ، وأراد المصنف من قوله: "ألا ترى بيان النظير لوجود الكل في ضمن البعض، فكما أن عدم الجزئين علة تامة لعدم الكلّ مثل عدم الجزء الواحد، كذلك الاتيان بالكل سبب للامتثال بالواجب، مثل الاتيان بالواحد غير المعين، ففي العلية (كون عدم الجزئين علة تامة لعدم الكل) عدم مجموع الجزئين بدل عن عدم جزء واحد في كون عدم المجموع علة تامة لعدم الكلّ، فهنا أيضاً الامتثال بالكل بدل عن الامتثال بالبعض.
فالواجب في صورة الامتثال (بالكل حقيقة واحد لا بعينه، فإذا وجد واحد معين فهو الواجب لاشتماله على أحد الكل .
وأجاب في المنهاج" : بأن الامتثال بكل ، وتلك معرفات، وفيه نظر ظاهر
وأشار إلى الجواب باختيار الشق الثاني (وهو الامتثال بكل واحد فنقل كلام البيضاوى عن المنهاج وقال : وأجاب في المنهاج بأن الامتثال بكل (واحد) ولا بأس بتعدد العلة التامة هنا، إذ تلك الأمور معرفات شرعية وليست بعلل حقيقية، حتى يلزم توارد العلل المستقلة على معلول واحد .
وقوله : وفيه نظر ظاهر : إشارة الاعتراض، وهو أن كلا من الاطعام والكسوة، والتحرير موجب مؤثر على سبيل البدلية في الامتثال، فلا فرق بينها وبين العلل العقلية التي يسمونها عللا حقيقية، فكل واحد علة مؤثرة في وجوب الامتثال والمجموع علل موجبة مؤثرة
The three: Secondly, if he performs all together, then compliance is either with all, so all become obligatory, or with each one, so multiple complete causes follow, or with one unspecified, and that does not exist, so the specific one is determined. I say: The obligation of all does not necessarily follow from compliance with all; it would only follow if it were not a substitute. Do you not see that the absence of a part is a complete cause for the absence of the whole; so if two parts are absent, the sum is the complete cause. The author replied saying: I say (first) choosing the first possibility that the obligation of all does not follow from compliance with all, because the whole is an individual of the concept of one of the three, and its existence within the three [is actual]; it would only follow what you said of the obligation of all from compliance with it if the whole were not a substitute for an unspecified one, but here the whole is a substitute for an unspecified one. Then he mentioned the illuminating proof and said: Do you not see that the absence of a part is a complete cause for the absence of the whole; so if two parts are absent, the sum is the complete cause. The author intended by his saying 'Do you not see' to explain the analogy of the existence of the whole within the part. Just as the absence of two parts is a complete cause for the absence of the whole like the absence of one part, similarly performing all is a cause for compliance with the obligatory like performing the one unspecified. So in the causality (the absence of two parts being a complete cause for the absence of the whole), the absence of the sum of the two parts is a substitute for the absence of one part in that the absence of the sum is a complete cause for the absence of the whole. Here also compliance with the whole is a substitute for compliance with some. Thus the obligatory in the case of compliance (with the whole) is in reality one unspecified; so when a specific one appears, it is the obligatory because it includes one of the whole. He answered in al-Minhaj: that compliance with each [is valid] and those are definers, and there is apparent objection. And he pointed to the answer by choosing the second possibility (which is compliance with each one). He transmitted the words of al-Baydawi from al-Minhaj and said: He answered in al-Minhaj that compliance with each (one) and there is no harm in multiple complete causes here, because those matters are Shariah definers and are not real causes such that independent causes converge on a single effect. And his saying: 'and there is apparent objection' is an indication of the objection, which is that each of feeding, clothing, and freeing is an effective cause on a substitutional basis for compliance, so there is no difference between them and intellectual causes which they call real causes; so each one is an effective cause in the obligation of compliance, and the sum are effective obligating causes.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents a usul dispute within the Hanafi school regarding the nature of compliance when a mukallaf performs all specified alternatives (such as kaffarah options) simultaneously. The key question is: if someone performs all three alternatives (feeding, clothing, freeing), does the obligation become fulfilled by all, by each, or by a single indeterminate one? The author, Badakhshani, first states the three possibilities and then refutes the view that performing all obligates all. He uses an analogy: just as the absence of two parts is a complete cause for the absence of the whole (like the absence of one part), similarly performing all is a substitute for performing one unspecified. Thus the obligation is fulfilled by one specific alternative that emerges. He then critiques the contradictory view from al-Minhaj, which claims that multiple complete causes are acceptable because these are 'definers' (mu'arrafat) not real causes. Badakhshani rejects this, arguing that each alternative is an effective cause for compliance on a substitutional basis, so they are analogous to real causes. The passage refines the understanding of badal (substitute) and the unity of the wajib.

Key Terms
بَدَل (badal): A substitute; here the whole set of alternatives is a substitute for an unspecified single alternative.
عِلَّة تَامَّة (illah tammah): A complete cause that necessarily produces its effect; here used analogically for causation in compliance.
امْتِثَال (imtithāl): Compliance with a command by performing the wajib act.
مُعَرِّف (mu'arraf): A definer; in usul, something that identifies or defines a ruling without being a real cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Minhaj view as critiqued in the passage, why are multiple complete causes acceptable when performing all alternatives?
A) Because the alternatives are different in nature, so they do not interfere.
B) Because the alternatives are 'definers' (mu'arrafat), not real causes.
C) Because the command is ambiguous and allows multiple interpretations.
D) Because there is no single cause that can unite them.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, when a wajib has alternatives (badal), performing all does not create multiple obligations; the set is a substitute for one unspecified wajib, so a single specific alternative fulfills it.

Do Composite Acts Form Multiple Causes or Identifiers?
Inter-madhhab dispute on composite obligation causality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi position that each alternative act in a composite obligation is an independent complete cause, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument that they are mere identifiers.

Core Idea
In composite obligations, each alternative is a complete cause; the obligatory is one unspecified.

This passage examines a key usul dispute about the nature of composite obligations (واجب مخير), such as the three kaffarah options. The Hanafi school holds that each prescribed act is a complete cause (علة تامة) for fulfilling the obligation, while the Shafi'i view treats them as Shar'i identifiers (معرفات). The debate hinges on whether multiple independent causes can concur on a single effect in Shar'i matters. Let's walk through the arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Issue
Pinpoint the core disagreement: Are the alternative acts in a composite obligation multiple complete causes or mere identifiers?
The kaffarah for breaking a fast intentionally: feed 60 poor, or clothe them, or free a slave.
2
State the Hanafi Position
The Hanafi view: each act is a complete cause (علة تامة) for the obligation; the obligatory is one unspecified act.
Analogous to how non-existence of the whole is a cause for non-existence of parts.
3
Examine the Shafi'i Argument from al-Minhaj
The Shafi'i claim: these acts are Shar'i identifiers, not real causes, because multiple true causes cannot produce one effect.
They invoke the principle of توارد العلل المستقلة على معلول واحد (concurrence of independent causes on one effect).
4
Analyze the Hanafi Response
Hanafi rebuttal: each act is مؤثر (effective) on substitution, just like real causes; no logical impossibility in Shari'ah.
The text explicitly states: كلا من الاطعام والكسوة والتحرير موجب مؤثر على سبيل البدلية.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Practical consequence: For Hanafis, performing any one act fulfills the unspecified obligation; for Shafi'is, one must intend a specific act, but the obligation is not composite in the same sense.
This affects how we conceptualize the wajib in mukhthayyarat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجزئين بدل عن عدم جزء واحد في كون عدم المجموع علة تامة لعدم الكلّ، فهنا أيضاً الامتثال بالكل بدل عن الامتثال بالبعض. فالواجب في صورة الامتثال بالكل حقيقة واحد لا بعينه، فإذا وجد واحد معين فهو الواجب لاشتماله على أحد الكل. وأجاب في المنهاج: بأن الامتثال بكل، وتلك معرفات، وفيه نظر ظاهر. وأشار إلى الجواب باختيار الشق الثاني (وهو الامتثال بكل واحد فنقل كلام البيضاوى عن المنهاج وقال: وأجاب في المنهاج بأن الامتثال بكل (واحد) ولا بأس بتعدد العلة التامة هنا، إذ تلك الأمور معرفات شرعية وليست بعلل حقيقية، حتى يلزم توارد العلل المستقلة على معلول واحد. وقوله: وفيه نظر ظاهر: إشارة الاعتراض، وهو أن كلا من الاطعام والكسوة والتحرير موجب مؤثر على سبيل البدلية في الامتثال، فلا فرق بينها وبين العلل العقلية التي يسمونها عللا حقيقية، فكل واحد علة مؤثرة في وجوب الامتثال والمجموع علل موجبة مؤثرة.
The two parts are a substitute for the absence of a single part, in that the non-existence of the whole is a complete cause for the non-existence of the entire thing. So here also, compliance with the whole is a substitute for compliance with the part. The obligatory in the case of compliance with the whole is truly one but not specified. When a specific one occurs, it is the obligatory because it includes one of the entire thing. And it was answered in al-Minhaj: that compliance is with each, and those are identifiers, and it contains apparent weakness. And he pointed to the answer by choosing the second branch (which is compliance with each one). He transmitted the words of al-Baydawi from al-Minhaj and said: It was answered in al-Minhaj that compliance is with each (one) and there is no harm in multiplicity of complete causes here, since those things are Shar'i identifiers and not real causes, such that independent causes would entail concurrence on a single effect. And his statement: 'and it contains apparent weakness' is an indication of the objection, which is that each of feeding, clothing, and freeing is an effective cause on the basis of substitution in compliance, so there is no difference between them and the intellectual causes which they call real causes. So each one is an effective cause in the obligation of compliance, and the totality are effective positive causes.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute about the nature of composite obligations (واجب مخير). The Hanafi position holds that when multiple acts are prescribed as alternatives (e.g., kaffarah), each act is a complete cause (علة تامة) for fulfilling the obligation, but the obligatory act is one unspecified. The text begins by stating that just as the non-existence of the whole is a cause for the non-existence of the parts, here compliance with the whole substitutes for compliance with a part. The obligatory act in compliance with the whole is one unspecified; when a specific act is performed, it becomes the obligatory. The Shafi'i argument from al-Minhaj claims that each act is merely a Shar'i identifier (معرف شرعي), not a real cause, thus multiple complete causes do not conflict. The Hanafi response, via a comment from al-Baydawi, rejects this: each of the three acts (feeding, clothing, freeing) is effective on the basis of substitution, so they are like real causes. Therefore each is an independent effective cause, and the totality is a set of effective causes. This upholds the Hanafi view that composite obligations involve multiple real causes that are alternative to each other.

Key Terms
علة تامة (illah tammah): A complete cause that necessarily produces its effect; in usul, a cause sufficient for the existence of a ruling.
معرف شرعي (mu'arraf shar'i): A Shar'i identifier that points to a ruling without being a real efficient cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, when performing a composite obligation (such as kaffarah), what is the legal status of each prescribed act (e.g., feeding, clothing, freeing)?
A) Each act is a mere identifier (معرف شرعي) that points to the same ruling without being a cause.
B) Each act is a complete cause (علة تامة) that is effective on the basis of substitution.
C) Only the first act chosen becomes the cause, and the others are invalid.
D) The acts are not causes but conditions for validity.

Key Takeaway
In composite obligations, the Hanafi school treats each alternative as an independent effective cause, so performing any one fulfills the single unspecified wajib.

Four Opinions on Wajib in an Expanded Time
Presents four madhhab views on the timing of wajib in a broad time frame

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four scholarly positions on the timing of wajib in an expanded time (al-wājib al-mūs‘a) and state the Hanafi condition that determines whether an early performance counts as wajib or nafl.

Core Idea
The Hanafi view ties the status of early performance to remaining mukallaf until the end of the time.

This passage from Izalat al-Rahbut presents four distinct positions on what exactly is required in each part of an expanded time for a wajib act. The issue is not merely theoretical; it affects whether a prayer performed early is considered ada', qada', or nafl. The Hanafi view, represented by al-Karkhi, offers a unique condition that reconciles flexibility with the certainty of obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four positions
List the four madhahib as presented: (1) Al-Qadi and most Shafi'is: act or resolve in every part, initial resolve suffices. (2) Al-Baqillani and most Shafi'is: act or resolve in every part, resolve extends from first to last. (3) Some Shafi'is/mutakallimun: must be at the very beginning; delay makes it qada. (4) Some Hanafis (al-Karkhi): true time is the end; earlier performance is conditionally wajib or nafl.
Note that the first two differ on whether the resolve is renewed or extended.
2
Analyze the Hanafi position
Focus on the fourth opinion: the time for actual performance is the very end. Early performance is nafl unless the person remains mukallaf until the end. Al-Karkhi's condition: if the person remains with the description of obligation (not insane, not menstruating, not unconscious) until the end, then what they performed earlier is counted as wajib. If they lose obligation before the end, it is nafl.
This condition prevents the paradox of performing an act that later becomes impossible to repeat as qada.
3
Examine the proof for the majority view
The passage then gives the proof (dalil) for the first opinion (majority of Shafi'is and theologians). The Lawgiver expanded the time, meaning performance in any part is valid by consensus. Specifying a particular part or requiring a choice between act and resolve would be contrary to expansion and an addition to the text.
This proof is cut off in the passage; we only have the beginning.
4
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: In the Hanafi view, if a person performs Zuhr early in its time and then goes insane before the end, that prayer is considered nafl (they are not obligated to repeat it as qada). In the Shafi'i view, it was wajib from the start and remains wajib regardless of subsequent loss of obligation.
This affects whether one should make up the prayer if obligation returns, e.g., after recovery from insanity.
5
Evaluate the strengths and critiques
The third position (must be at the beginning) is criticized for contradicting the hadith that divides prayer time into three parts and for making the wajib lose its place. The Hanafi position avoids these issues by conditioning the status on remaining mukallaf, while the majority view relies on the literal expansion of time.
Each position has its usul basis; the Hanafi position emphasizes the end as the moment of actual obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إزالة الرهبوت عن مشكلات مسلم الثبوت
قال القاضي وأكثر الشافعية : الواجب في كل وقت الفعل، أو العزم بدلاً (عنه) ويتعين إتيان (الفعل) أخيراً، ولا يوجبون تجديد العزم في كل جزء، بل الأول ينسحب انسحاب النية، فلا يرد ما في "المنهاج أن البدل متعدد والمبدل منه واحد على أن إيقاعات الفعل بعدد الأجزاء فتساوى الأعزام
- والثاني مذهب القاضى أبي بكر الباقلاني وأكثر الشافعية
أن الواجب في كل أجزاء) الوقت اتيان الفعل الواجب أو العزم وهو (وارادة) اتيانه بدلاً عنه، ويتعين آخراً ( في الجزء الآخر) إتيان الفعل
وبما أن العزم (والارادة) يمتد من الجزء الأول، إلى آخر الجزء كامتداد النية (نية الصوم من أول اليوم إلى آخره لا حاجة إلى إيجاب تجديد العزم في كل جزء، فكما أن المبدل ،(الفعل) ،واحد كذلك البدل (العزم) أيضاً يكون واحداً، فلا يرد الإشكال المذكور في المنهاج للبيضاوى من أن البدل العزم الجديد في كل جزء) متعدد والمبدل منه ( وهو الفعل) واحد ، فلم يوجد التوافق بين البدل والمبدل منه ؟ وجه عدم الورود هو عدم تعدد العزم ، بل امتد عزم الجزء الأول إلى الآخر، وأشار إلى الجواب الثاني وقال: على أن ايقاعات الفعل في الجزء الأول أو الوسط أو الآخر) بعدد الأجزاء أجزاء الوقت فتساوى عدد ايقاعات الفعل المبدل عدد الأعزام البدل، يعنى لو سلمنا أن العزم متعدد، فايقاعات الفعل باعتبار أجزاء الوقت أيضاً متعددة فتوافقا.
وعن بعض الشافعية ، وقيل عن بعض المتكلمين وقته أوله ، فإن أخره فقضاء
-٣- والثالث : مذهب بعض الشافعية بل بعض المتكلمين : وهو أن وقت الواجب أوّل الوقت الموسع لسبقته، فإن أخره المكلف عن أول وقته فهو قضاء لا أداء، وهذا خطاء من وجهين : الأول انه خلاف نص الحديث الذى أثبت لوقت الصلاة ثلاثة أجزاء: (الأول) والوسط والآخر) والثاني أنه لو صار قضاء بالتأخير لم يبق الواجب موضعاً.
وعن بعض الحنفية ، بل الوقت آخره فإن قدمه فنقل يسقط به الفرض قال الكرخي إن بقى بصفة التكليف إلى آخر الوقت فما قدمه فهو واجب
والرابع : مذهب بعض الحنفية
وهو أن وقت أداء الواجب هو آخر الوقت؛ لأن الأداء صحيح في آخره، ولا يلزمه القضاء، وهذا معنى قوله: "بل (الوقت) آخره ثم تفزع ذلك البعض على مذهبه وقال: فإن قدمه (قدم الواجب على آخر الوقت فهو نفل يسقط به الفرض ( عن المكلف).
مستند المذهب الرابع: قال أبو الحسن الكرخى : إن بقى المكلف بصفة التكليف إلى آخر الوقت (بأن لم يصر مجنوناً، وما جاء المرأة حيض أو نفاس، ولم يغم عليه) فما قدمه (جاء به قبيل آخر الوقت فهو من الواجب .
وإذا لم يبق إلى آخر الوقت مكلفاً فما قدمه فهو نفل .
دليل المذهب الأول (الجمهور)
لنا أن الآمر وسع وقت الفعل ؛ لأنه لو أتى في أي جزء لا يعد عاصياً بالإجماع، والتعيين تضييق والتخيير بين الفعل والعزم زيادة
والدليل لنا : أن الشارع الأمر جعل وقت الفعل الواجب موسعاً ؛ للإشارة إلى أن المكلف لو أتى بالواجب في أى جزء من الوقت كان ممتثلاً، ولا يعد عاصياً بالاجماع، وأما تعيين بعض أجزاء الوقت للأداء فتضييق (وهو خلاف التوسيع) وأما التخيير بين الفعل والعزم (نية الأداء) فزيادة على النص ؛ لأن النص الدال على التوسيع في الوقت ليس فيه هذا التخيير .
Removing the Dread from the Problems of Muslim al-Thubut. Al-Qadi and most Shafi'is said: The wajib in every time is the act, or the resolve as a substitute for it, and performing the act becomes determined finally, and they do not require renewing the resolve in every part; rather the first extends like the extension of intention, so what is mentioned in al-Minhaj that the substitute is multiple and the substituted is one does not arise, because the occurrences of acts are according to the number of parts, so they equal the resolves. - The second is the madhhab of al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani and most Shafi'is: That the wajib in every part of the time is to bring the wajib act or the resolve, which is the intention to do it as a substitute for it, and it becomes determined finally (in the last part) to bring the act. And since the resolve (and intention) extends from the first part to the last part like the extension of intention (intention of fasting from the beginning of the day to its end), there is no need to require renewing the resolve in every part; so just as the substituted (the act) is one, so the substitute (the resolve) is also one. Thus the objection mentioned in al-Minhaj by al-Baydawi that the substitute (the new resolve in every part) is multiple and the substituted (the act) is one does not arise. The reason there is no objection is that the resolve is not multiple; rather the resolve of the first part extends to the end. And he pointed to the second answer saying: Since the occurrences of the act (in the first, middle, or last part) are according to the number of parts of the time, so the number of occurrences of the act (the substitute) equals the number of resolves (the substitute). Meaning if we grant that the resolve is multiple, then the occurrences of the act according to the parts of time are also multiple, so they match. - And from some Shafi'is, and it is said from some theologians: its time is the beginning; if he delays it, it is qada. - 3- The third: The madhhab of some Shafi'is, rather some theologians: That the time of the wajib is the beginning of the expanded time because it precedes, and if the mukallaf delays it from its first time, it is qada, not ada'. And this is an error from two aspects: First, it contradicts the text of the hadith that established for the time of prayer three parts: the first, the middle, and the last. Second, if it becomes qada by delay, the wajib would have no place. - And from some Hanafis: Rather the time is its end; if he performs it early, it is nafl that fulfills the obligation. Al-Karkhi said: If he remains with the description of obligation until the end of the time, then what he did earlier is wajib. - The fourth: The madhhab of some Hanafis: That the time of performing the wajib is the end of the time; because performance is valid at its end, and he is not liable to qada, and this is the meaning of his saying: "Rather the time is its end." Then that group went further in their madhhab and said: If he performs it early (performs the wajib before the end of the time) it is nafl that fulfills the obligation (from the mukallaf). - The basis of the fourth madhhab: Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi said: If the mukallaf remains with the description of obligation until the end of the time (like not becoming insane, no menstruation or post-partum bleeding for women, and no fainting), then what he performed (brought before the end of the time) is from the wajib. And if he does not remain mukallaf until the end of the time, what he performed is nafl. - The proof of the first madhhab (the majority): For us, the Commander expanded the time of the act; because if he performs in any part, he is not considered disobedient by consensus. And specification is a restriction, and the choice between act and resolve is an addition. And the proof for us: That the Lawgiver made the time of the wajib act expanded; to indicate that if the mukallaf were to perform the wajib in any part of the time, he would be compliant, and he is not considered disobedient by consensus. As for specifying some parts of the time for performance, it is a restriction (and it contradicts expansion). As for the choice between act and resolve (intention of performance), it is an addition to the text; because the text indicating expansion in time does not contain this choice.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage presents four scholarly opinions (madhahib) regarding the nature of obligation (wajib) when the time for its performance is expanded (waktu musa'). The first opinion, attributed to al-Qadi (Ibn al-Tayyib) and most Shafi'is, holds that in every part of the time, the wajib is either the act itself or the resolve (azm) to perform it as a substitute. However, they do not require renewing the resolve in each part; the initial resolve suffices throughout. The second opinion, from al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani and most Shafi'is, similarly requires the act or resolve in each part, but the resolve extends from the first part to the last, like the intention for fasting. The third opinion, from some Shafi'is and theologians (mutakallimun), is that the wajib must be performed at the very beginning of the time; delaying it makes it qada, not ada. The author criticizes this as contradicting the hadith that divides prayer time into three parts and rendering the wajib without a proper place. The fourth opinion is the Hanafi view (attributed to some Hanafis, notably Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi): the true time for performance is the very end of the expanded period. If the individual remains mukallaf (i.e., not insane, menstruating, or unconscious) until that end, then any act performed earlier (before the end) is considered wajib—it discharges the obligation. But if the person loses obligation status before the end (e.g., dies, goes insane), the earlier act becomes nafl, not wajib. The passage then begins the proof (dalil) for the first (majority) view: the Lawgiver intentionally expanded the time to allow performance in any part, so specifying a part or adding a choice between act and resolve would be an unwarranted restriction or addition to the text. This proof is cut off at the end. Note that the Hanafi position (fourth) is distinctive: it defines the wajib as what is performed when one remains mukallaf until the end; earlier acts are conditionally wajib. This has practical implications for intention and timeliness in acts like prayer.

Key Terms
الواجب الموسع (al-wājib al-mūs‘a): An obligation whose performance time is broadened, allowing performance at any point within a defined interval.
العزم (al-‘azm): The firm intention or resolve to perform the wajib act, which in the first two opinions can substitute for the actual act in each part of the time.
النفل (al-nafl): A supererogatory or voluntary act; in the Hanafi view, an obligatory act performed early in an expanded time is considered nafl if the person later loses obligation status before the end.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view (fourth opinion), what determines whether an act performed early in an expanded time counts as wajib or nafl?
A) Whether the person intended it as nafl or wajib
B) Whether the person remains mukallaf (obligated) until the end of the time
C) Whether the person performs it in the first or last part of the time
D) The majority opinion of the scholars

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi view on wajib in an expanded time uniquely conditions the status of early performance on remaining under obligation until the end, blending flexibility with precision.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
8 min
The Obligation in Prayer: Act or One of Two? – Hanafi vs. Bāqillānī
Usul dispute on the nature of obligation in prayer: Hanafi vs. Bāqillānī

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on the obligator in prayer, explain the opposing argument of al-Bāqillānī, and evaluate the critiques of the consensus claim.

Core Idea
The command to pray is specific to the act, not a choice between action and resolve.

This passage delves into a foundational usul dispute: when Allah commands prayer, is the obligation fulfilled by the act itself, or is there an alternative obligation of resolve (al-‘azm) that also counts? The Hanafi school insists on the act, while al-Qāḍī al-Bāqillānī and his followers argue that one of two things—either the act or the resolve—suffices. Understanding this debate sharpens your ability to analyze the nature of commands in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State the Hanafi position: the obligator is the act of prayer. State the Bāqillānī position: the obligator is one of two (act or resolve).
This is the core ikhtilāf.
2
Examine the Hanafi proof from the Quran
The Hanafi side cites the verse 'Aqim al-salāh' (17:78). In Hanafi usul, a command (amr) indicates obligation (wujūb). The commanded object is specifically 'al-salāh', i.e., the act of prayer, not a hypothetical choice.
Wajh al-dalālah: the imperative demands the specific act.
3
Understand the opponent's argument
Bāqillānī claims that the one praying in a part other than the last part is compliant not because he performs the act, but because he does one of two (act or resolve). They argue that if someone resolves at the beginning, he is not sinful even if he delays.
This challenges the Hanafi understanding of compliance.
4
Analyze the consensus claim and its refutation
The Hanafi side initially invokes consensus (ijmā') that compliance is by the act. The author refutes this by noting that the consensus is only established for the last part of the prayer, not for every part. Hence the consensus claim fails to support the premise.
This is a critical refinement of the argument.
5
Evaluate the final resolution
The author distinguishes between badaliyya (full substitutability, like expiation options) and aṣl-khalaf (original-substitute, like wudū' and tayammum). Bāqillānī's position is of the latter type: the act is the original, resolve is a substitute. Therefore, compliance with the act (the original) does not undermine their position; rather, they accept that the act is primary.
This clarifies that the dispute is not about the importance of the act but about whether resolve alone can be a valid alternative.

واستدل على خلاف مذهب القاضى وأتباعه من أكثر الشافعية بأن المصلى في غير الجزء الآخر ممتثل للأمر بالأداء لكونه مصلياً قطعاً، لا لكونه آيتاً بأحد الأمرين (بالفعل أو العزم ) كما زعم القاضي واتباعه، فلو عبارة عن الاتيان بمثل ما أمر به الأمر، والشارع الأمر إنما أمر بالصلاة في قوله : ((أقم الصلاة لدلوك (الشمس)) ولم يأمره بأحد الأمرين (الفعل أو العزم فيكون المصلى في غير الجزء الآخر ممتثلاً لأجل أنه أتى بالصلاة، لا بما لم يأمره وهو أحد الأمرين. ثم أقول : الخصم لا يقول بالبدلية من الطرفين، كخصال الكفارة، بل أصل وخلف، فالامتثال بالصلاة بخصوصها لا يضره ثم أقول الخصم (القاضي وأتباعه ( لا يقول بالبدلية من And he argued against the position of al-Qāḍī and his followers, who are most of the Shāfi‘īs, that the one praying in a part other than the last part is compliant with the command to perform because he is certainly praying, not because he is doing one of two things (act or resolve) as al-Qāḍī and his followers claimed. For if the obligation were one of the two, he would be compliant by doing one of the two, without prayer, whereas he certainly performed prayer. And it was said from the side of al-Qāḍī and his followers: We do not concede that the one praying in a part other than the last part is compliant because he is praying; rather, he is compliant because he is doing one of the two things. Indeed, that one is the act, not the resolve. And this is the meaning of his saying: 'Perhaps he denies the premise that the one praying in a part other than the last part is compliant because he is doing the act, not because he is doing one of the two; since his being a prayer is his doing one of the two.' The response to that denial: It was said in response that it (that premise) is subject to consensus decisively, and they have agreed that the one praying in a part other than the last part is compliant because he is praying, not because he is doing one of the two, even if that one is the act. Then the author wanted to refute that response and said: I say: The consensus is held on compliance with it (prayer) specifically in every part, and the disagreement (and the mention of schools) has preceded in this matter. Thus the denial of the premise is valid. The conclusion: Since the consensus on its obligation in every part other than the last part was not established, the consensus on compliance with it specifically therein was not established. Thus the denial of the premise that the one praying is compliant in the part other than the last part by the act (prayer) not by one of the two (act or resolve) is valid. And his saying 'reflect' is an indication of a response to that denial in another style, so that the problem does not arise. The gist of the response is that the one praying in a part other than the last part is compliant because he is praying, not because he is doing one of the two commands; for compliance is the doing of the like of what was commanded, and the Lawgiver commanded prayer in His statement: 'Establish prayer at the decline of the sun' and did not command him one of the two (act or resolve). Thus the one praying in a part other than the last part is compliant because he performed prayer, not because of what he was not commanded, which is one of the two. Then I say: The opponent does not say with substitution from both sides, like the expiation options, but rather original and substitute, so compliance with prayer specifically does not harm him. Then I say: The opponent (al-Qāḍī and his followers) does not say with substitution from both sides, like the expiation options, but rather original and substitute, like ablution and dry ablution. So compliance with prayer specifically does not harm him. It does not harm the opponent, for the opponent does not say that act and resolve are each a substitute for the other, like feeding, clothing, and freeing in expiation options, where each is a substitute for the other in the validity of compliance and permissibility of performance. As for act and resolve, one of them (act) is original and the other (resolve and intention) is a branch, like ablution and dry ablution. So compliance with prayer, which is the original, does not harm the opponent in his evidence. Evidence of al-Qāḍī and his followers for their school: They said: If one performs one of the two, it suffices, and if he neglects both, he sins. We say: The sin is denied; how often is there no act or its intention at the beginning of the time? And if it is said: What is meant is the absence of intention to abandon, we say: That is from the rulings of faith. Do you not see if...
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a disputation in usul al-fiqh concerning the nature of the obligation in prayer. The Hanafi position (implied) is that the obligator (al-wājib) is the prayer itself, not a choice between act and resolve. The opponent is al-Qāḍī Abū Bakr al-Bāqillānī and his followers, who hold that the obligation is one of two: either the actual performance of prayer or the resolve/intention to perform it. The argument presented first is that the one praying is certainly performing the act, so he is complying with the command to pray, not with a command to do one of two things. The opponent replies that the compliance is due to doing one of the two, specifically the act. The Hanafi side then invokes consensus (ijmā') that compliance is due to being a musallī (performer of prayer), not due to performing one of two. The author (the commentator) refutes this by noting that such consensus is not established for every part of the prayer, only for the last part (the part that completes the obligation). Then the commentator offers a deeper response: compliance means doing what was commanded, and what was commanded is prayer (verse 'Aqim al-salāh'), not one of two. The opponent's claim that the obligation is one of two is not based on substitutability (badaliyya) like expiation options, but rather on the relation of original (aṣl) and substitute (khalaf), like ablution and dry ablution. Hence, compliance with the original act does not conflict with their position; they accept that the prayer itself is the original, but if one intends to pray from the beginning of time, he is also compliant. The opponent's own evidence is: if one does one of the two, it suffices, and if he omits both, he sins. The Hanafi side counters that 'sin' is not established because at the beginning of the time one may not have the act or its intention, yet not be sinful. The text cuts off before completing the argument about faith.

Key Terms
امتثال (imtithāl): Compliance with a command by performing the commanded action.
بدلية (badaliyya): Substitutability where each option is an alternative for the other in fulfilling the obligation.
أصل وخلف (aṣl wa khalaf): Original and substitute: one is primary, the other secondary (like wudū' and tayammum).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the object of the command in prayer?
A) Either the act of prayer or the intention to pray.
B) The act of prayer itself.
C) The intention to pray only.
D) Both the act and intention simultaneously.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position is that the obligator in prayer is the act itself, derived from the precise wording of the command; opposing views that introduce alternatives must be scrutinized for their evidence.

Does Intention Substitute Action in Expanded Obligations? A Hanafi Critique
Usul dispute: does intention substitute action in expanded duties?

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on intention (azm) in wajib musa' and evaluate the opposing view's proof using usul principles.

Core Idea
Intention alone never substitutes for action; the commanded act must be performed.

In this passage, Al-Badakhshani refutes a claim by al-Qadi and his followers that in an expanded obligatory act (wajib musa'), having a firm intention (azm) to perform the act at any point within the time period suffices as fulfillment of the obligation. The Hanafi school insists that the act itself must be performed; intention is a condition but not a substitute. This lesson walks through the dispute, the opponent's proof, and the Hanafi refutation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
What is the key question being debated? The text addresses whether intention (azm) to perform an expanded wajib (e.g., prayer within its time) substitutes for actual performance, or whether the act itself is required.
Also note the opponent clarifies they do not view action and intention as equal substitutes; one is original, the other subsidiary.
2
State the opponent's proof
The opponent argues: if the mukallaf performs either the act or the intention, it suffices; if he omits both, he sins. They base this on the idea that the obligation is to do one of the two.
The opponent uses an analogy with wudu and tayammum, where tayammum is a valid substitute when wudu is impossible.
3
Analyze the Hanafi refutation
The author refutes both aspects: (1) intention does not suffice because the text commands the act itself; (2) omission of both does not automatically entail sin, as one may intend but be prevented and later perform without sin.
The Hanafi approach is to uphold the literal command and reject any relaxation of the requirement.
4
Examine the opponent's redefinition
The opponent attempts to salvage their argument by redefining 'determination' as 'not intending abandonment' rather than intending the act. They claim this is always present.
The author counters that this non-intention of abandonment is already a ruling of faith (iman), not a specific act of obedience to the commanded duty.
5
Synthesize the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if a person intends to pray but dies before the end of the time, the opponent says he is not sinful, but the Hanafis consider him sinful for not performing the act.
This difference highlights the strictness of the Hanafi position on obligation.

And if it is said (in correcting the proof and answering this refutation): What is meant by determination is not intending abandonment, meaning determination does not mean intending the action so that the refuter's statement 'How often is action or its intention not present at the beginning of the time' would be valid. Rather, what is meant by determination is not intending abandonment. So at the beginning or middle of the time, even if action or determination in the sense of intention is not present, yet one of the two things has been found, namely determination in the sense of not intending abandonment, for it has been found from the obligated person that he does not intend to abandon the action. So one of the two things is found, namely determination in the sense of not intending abandonment, and non-disobedience is realized. Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This is a usul dispute concerning the nature of obligation in a wajib musa' (expanded obligatory act). The opponent, identified as al-Qadi and his followers, argues that the mukallaf is obligated to either perform the commanded act or have a determination (azm) to perform it at any point within the time period. If he does either, it suffices; if he omits both, he sins. The author, Al-Badakhshani, refutes this view, presenting the Hanafi position. He first clarifies that the opponent does not view the two options as mutual substitutes like the expiatory alternatives (feeding, clothing, freeing) but rather one is the original (action) and the other is a successor (determination), similar to wudu and tayammum. Thus, compliance with the original (prayer) does not harm the opponent's argument. However, the author then attacks the opponent's proof on two fronts: First, performing determination alone does not satisfy the obligation because the Lawgiver commanded the act itself, not determination. Determination is merely a condition and must be followed by the actual performance by the end of the time. Second, not performing either does not automatically constitute sin, as one may have determination at the start but be prevented by circumstances, then perform later without sin. The opponent attempts to salvage the argument by redefining 'determination' as 'not intending abandonment' rather than intending the act. The author counters that this non-intent to abandon is already a ruling of faith (iman) and not a specific act of obedience to the commanded duty, thus it cannot serve as a substitute. The debate underscores the Hanafi strictness: the commanded act itself must be performed; mental resolution alone does not discharge the obligation.

Key Terms
الواجب الموسع (al-wājib al-muwassa‘): An obligatory act whose performance is required within a broad time frame (e.g., the five daily prayers), where the obligation is to perform the act at some point within that time.
العزم (al-‘azm): Determination or firm intention to perform the commanded act at some future point within the time.
الامتثال (al-imtithāl): Compliance with the command by performing what was ordered.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position presented in this passage, what is the ruling on a person who intends to pray at the beginning of the prayer time but does not actually pray until the very end due to sleep?
A) He is sinful because he delayed the prayer without valid excuse.
B) He is not sinful because he had the intention at the beginning.
C) He is not sinful because he performed the prayer before the time expired.
D) He is sinful unless he intended to pray at every moment.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, intention (azm) is a condition for valid worship but never a substitute for the commanded act itself.

Why Determination Cannot Substitute for the Act in Expanded Obligations
Refuting the view that determination (azm) substitutes for the act in an expanded obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the Hanafi position that 'azm cannot substitute for the act in an expanded wajib, evaluate al-Baqillani's counter-argument, and apply the distinction between expanded and restricted obligations.

Core Idea
Determination ('azm) is a sign of faith, not a substitute for the act; the act remains obligatory until performed.

This lesson unpacks a subtle debate in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: can a firm intention to perform an expanded duty substitute for the actual performance? The author refutes the view of al-Baqillani and some Hanafis by showing that 'azm is a necessary concomitant of faith, not a legal substitute, and that treating it as a substitute leads to logical absurdities. We will walk through the proofs and the practical consequences of the dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
What exactly is being refuted? The view that 'azm (determination) can serve as a badal (substitute) for the actual act in an expanded wajib. Note the distinction: the substitute could replace either the act or the obligation (wujub).
The text focuses on the act (fi'l), not the obligation.
2
Examine the first proof from sin before time
The author argues: if a person intends to abandon the commanded act before the time of obligation enters, he becomes a sinner. This shows that the lack of intention to abandon is not a ruling of the wajib but a sign of faith. Thus, 'azm cannot be a substitute because it is pre-obligation.
This proof uses the premise that sin attaches to the intention to abandon, which is independent of the obligation.
3
Analyze the 'substitute precedes the substituted' objection
If 'azm is a substitute for the act, it would have to precede the act. But a substitute logically follows the original. This is impossible, so the claim fails.
This is a logical (aqli) argument, not text-based.
4
Review al-Baqillani's response and the author's rebuttal
Al-Baqillani's followers argue that 'azm substitutes for the obligation (wujub), not the act, so the act need not fall. The author rebuts by noting that if 'azm removed the obligation, then delaying the act would not be sinful, but we know that in an expanded wajib the obligation persists until the end of the time. The distinction between expanded (muwassa') and restricted (mudayyaq) is crucial.
The author sides with the majority Hanafi position: the act itself remains obligatory; 'azm does not lift it.
5
Apply the expanded vs restricted distinction to the fourth Hanafi view
Some Hanafis argued that performance is only obligatory at the last moment. The author counters that this would only apply if the wajib were restricted; in an expanded wajib, the entire time is a container, so sin occurs if no intention and no act within the time.
This clarifies that the obligation attaches to the act, not to a specific moment.

Summary: The discussion is about the expanded obligatory act, and not intending to abandon it is among the rulings and signs of faith. For among the necessities of a believer and signs of his faith is not intending to abandon faith and the acts that are required by faith. Then he mentioned a proof and said: Do you not see that if the responsible person neglects the determination (with the intention of performance) by intending to abandon the commanded act before the time enters, and before performance becomes obligatory upon him, he becomes a sinner. So it is known that not intending to abandon is not from the rulings of the obligatory, but rather it is among the signs and necessities of faith—its sacredness even before obligation. And his saying (so understand) indicates another aspect for the impermissibility of making determination in the discussion of the obligatory meaning not intending to abandon; because not intending to abandon is a necessity before the time enters and before obligation, meaning before performing the obligatory, so it would necessitate the precedence of the substitute (and it is determination) in this meaning over the substituted (and it is the act), because the branch must be after the origin, not before it. The answer is denial of the necessary connection; rather the necessary consequence is the falling of the obligation, and they have committed to it. And he said: And in al-Badi' by al-Nizam al-Sa'ati: If determination were a substitute for the act, it would cause the substituted to fall like other substitutes. The summary of the problem: If determination were a substitute for the act, it would necessitate the falling of the act substituted by the determination which is a substitute for it, just as ablution falls with tayammum which is a substitute for it, while determination does not cause the act to fall. So its invalidity became clear. Then he mentioned the answer from the side of al-Qadi and his followers, and said: The answer is denial of the necessary connection (denying the necessity of the act falling by determination); rather the necessary consequence (according to them) is the falling of the obligation (not the act). And they have committed to it (i.e., al-Qadi and his followers made the falling of the obligation necessary upon them). The summary of the answer is that determination according to them in the expanded obligatory is a substitute for the obligation, not the act, and it is permissible for the obligation itself to fall by determination according to them. The proof of the fourth school—some Hanafis said: If performance were obligatory at first (at the beginning of time), he would become a sinner (the responsible person becomes a sinner) by delaying it to the middle or the end, so the time of performance is the last time only. This is the school of some Hanafis and their proof. The author mentioned the answer to it and said: We say: (His sinning by delaying) is denied; rather sin would be necessary if the obligatory were restricted and the time were a measure for it. Rather the obligatory here is expanded, and the time is a container for it. This is the discussion concerning it.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is a debate on whether the determination (azm) to perform an obligation can serve as a substitute (badal) for the actual act. The author begins by stating that not intending to abandon an obligation is a sign of faith, not a legal ruling of the wajib itself. He provides a proof: if a person intends to abandon the commanded act before the time of obligation enters, he becomes a sinner—proving that the lack of intention to abandon is a requirement of faith, not of the obligation. Then he critiques the idea of making 'azm a substitute for the act, because that would imply the substitute (azm) precedes the substituted (the act), which is impossible. The text cites al-Badi' to argue that if 'azm were a substitute, it would cause the act to fall, like tayammum causes wudu to fall—but this is not the case. The response from al-Qadi al-Baqillani and his followers is that 'azm instead causes the obligation (wujub) to fall, not the act. The author then refutes a fourth Hanafi view claiming that performance is only obligatory at the last moment, arguing that such sinning only applies to a restricted (mudayyaq) wajib, not an expanded (muwassa') one. The passage concludes by emphasizing that an expanded wajib has a time that is a container (zarf) for the act, not a measure (mi'yar).

Key Terms
عزم (azm): Determination or firm intention to perform an act
بدل (badal): Substitute that stands in for the original obligation and causes its requirement to fall
وجوب (wujub): The quality of being obligatory in the sight of the law

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because 'azm is not mentioned in the Quran.
B) Because 'azm would have to precede the act, which is impossible.
C) Because the act is not obligatory.
D) Because 'azm is only for faith, not for legal rulings.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, determination ('azm) cannot substitute for the act in an expanded wajib; the act remains obligatory until performed.

The Cause (Sabab) in the Expanded Obligation
Four madhhabs on the sabab of an expanded wajib

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the cause in an expanded wajib and evaluate how it differs from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
In an expanded wajib, the cause is the first part, but it extends flexibly until the last part.

When an obligation has a time window, what exactly triggers the duty? Is it the first moment, the entire window, or something else? This passage from a classical usul text presents four scholarly views on the cause (sabab) in an expanded obligatory act (wajib muwassa'), with particular emphasis on the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions. Understanding this concept is essential for grasping how obligations are structured over time in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its category
The subject is the 'wajib muwassa'' (expanded obligatory act), an obligation with a time window. Identify what kind of act is under discussion.
This is an usul al-fiqh definition, not a specific fiqh ruling.
2
List the four positions from the text
The author mentions four views: Shafi'i, general Hanafi, Zufar (implied), and a narration from Abu al-Yusr. Write each one briefly.
The text numbers them; ensure you capture all four.
3
Analyze the consensus proof
The text cites a proof from consensus: if a person converts to Islam or reaches puberty in the middle of the time, obligation is immediate. Consider how each school would interpret this proof.
This proof is used by Abu al-Yusr and can also support the Hanafi flexible position.
4
Compare the Hanafi and Shafi'i definitions of sabab
The Shafi'is hold the sabab is exclusively the first part. The Hanafis hold the sabab is the first part but extends to the last. Note the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).
The practical difference includes when a person becomes sinful for delaying.

مسألة السببية فى وقت الواجب الموسع السبب في الموسع الجزء الأول عينا (بعينه) عند الشافعية للسبق وعند عامة الحنفية، بل موسعًا إلى الأخير إلى ما يسع الأداء وبعد الخروج فالكل وروى عن أبي اليسر أن الأخير متعين حينئذ واستدل بالاجماع على الوجوب على من أسلم أو بلغ في وسط الوقت، ويمكن أن يقال أنه الأول في حقهما، فتدبر وذكر المصنف في هذه المسألة أربعة مذاهب : ١ - الأول مذهب الشافعية : السبب في الواجب الموسع (هو) الجزء الأول عيناً (متعيناً) عند الشافعية؛ للسبق (أي لسبقة ذلك الجزء على بقية الأجزاء) ۲ - والثاني مذهب الحنفية : وعند عامة الحنفية : بل الشبب هو الجزء الأول (حال كونه) موسعاً إلى الجزء الآخر، فإذا لم يؤدّ في الجزء الأول ينتقل السببية إلى آخر الجزء الممكن فيه الأداء) كما أن المسبب (الأداء) ينتقل بانتقال السبب إلى الثاني والثالث، أي يقع الأداء بالجزء الآخر الذي أراد المكلف الأداء فيه .
Issue of causality in the time of the expanded obligatory. The cause in the expanded (obligatory) is the first part specifically (by itself) according to the Shafi'is due to precedence; and according to the general Hanafis, rather it is expanded to the last (part), to what can accommodate performance; and after exit, then all. And it is narrated from Abu al-Yusr that the last becomes specified at that point. And they adduced consensus on obligation upon one who embraced Islam or reached puberty in the middle of the time. And it can be said that it is the first in their case, so ponder. And the author mentioned in this issue four schools: 1- The first is the school of the Shafi'is: the cause in the expanded obligatory is the first part specifically (determinately) according to the Shafi'is, due to precedence (i.e., the precedence of that part over the remaining parts). 2- The second is the school of the Hanafis: and according to the general Hanafis, rather the cause is the first part (while it is) expanded to the last part; if one does not perform in the first part, the causality transfers to the last part in which performance is possible, just as the caused (the performance) transfers with the transfer of the cause to the second and third, i.e., the performance occurs in the last part in which the responsible person intends to perform.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of the cause (sabab) that triggers the obligation in a time-expanded wajib. It presents four positions: (1) Shafi'i: the sabab is exclusively the first moment of the time. (2) General Hanafi: the sabab is the first moment but it is flexible, extending to the last moment if not performed initially. (3) Zufar: the sabab is the part that can accommodate the performance. (4) Narration from Abu al-Yusr: the last part becomes the specific cause when the time is about to end. The text also notes a proof from consensus: if a person converts to Islam or reaches puberty in the middle of the time, obligation is immediate—suggesting the first part for them. However, the Hanafi position allows transfer of causality. The author advises to ponder these views.

Key Terms
سبب (sabab): The temporal cause that brings about the obligation to perform the act
واجب موسع (wajib muwassa'): An obligation with a time window that can be performed at any point within the time
جزء أول (juz' awwal): The first part or moment of the time window

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the Hanafi position on the sabab of an expanded wajib?
A) The sabab is exclusively the last part of the time
B) The sabab is the first part but it is expanded to the last part
C) The sabab is any part of the time that the person chooses
D) The sabab is the middle part of the time

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that in an expanded obligatory act, the cause (sabab) is the first part of the time, but it is flexible and extends to the last part if performance is delayed.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

The Cause in Expanded Obligations: Four Madhhab Views
Exposition of four madhhab views on the cause in expanded obligations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four madhhab positions on the cause (sabab) of expanded obligations and evaluate the Hanafi proof from consensus.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school: the cause is the first part but extended, allowing transfer to later moments.

This passage from the Usul text presents a fundamental dispute among the madhahib: what exactly is the cause that triggers an expanded obligation like the five daily prayers? Is it the first moment, the last, or any accommodating part? The Hanafi view is nuanced, arguing that the cause is the first part but it is extended (musa'), transferring to subsequent parts if not performed immediately. This lesson unpacks the four positions and the evidence—particularly the consensus (ijma') argument—and shows how the author resolves the dispute by distinguishing a real first from a relative first.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four positions
List the four schools: Shafi'i, Hanafi majority, Zufar, Abu al-Yusr. For each, state their view on the sabab.
Shafi'i says first part specifically; Hanafi says first but extended; Zufar says to the part that accommodates; Abu al-Yusr says the last part when time exits.
2
Understand the Hanafi proof
The Hanafis argue by consensus: if a person becomes mukallaf in the middle of the time, all agree they must pray immediately. If the cause were only the real first, they wouldn't be obligated. So the cause must be the first but extended.
This proof relies on the agreement on obligation.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i rebuttal
The Shafi'is respond that for the newly obligated, the middle is their first, so the cause is still the first (relative). Thus the consensus does not prove extension.
This is a key nuance.
4
Reflect on the resolution
The author says 'fata dabbar' (so reflect), indicating that the real first vs relative first distinction resolves the dispute.
This shows the depth of usul analysis.

مسألة السببية فى وقت الواجب الموسع

السبب في الموسع الجزء الأول عينا (بعينه) عند الشافعية للسبق وعند عامة الحنفية، بل موسعًا إلى الأخير كالمسبب وعند زفر إلى ما يسع الأداء وبعد الخروج فالكل وروى عن أبي اليسر أن الأخير متعين حينئذ واستدل بالاجماع على الوجوب على من أسلم أو بلغ في وسط الوقت، ويمكن أن يقال أنه الأول في حقهما، فتدبر

وذكر المصنف في هذه المسألة أربعة مذاهب :
١ - الأول مذهب الشافعية : السبب في الواجب الموسع (هو) الجزء الأول عيناً (متعيناً) عند الشافعية؛ للسبق (أي لسبقة ذلك الجزء على بقية الأجزاء) 
٢ - والثاني مذهب الحنفية : وعند عامة الحنفية : بل الشبب هو الجزء الأول (حال كونه) موسعاً إلى الجزء الآخر، فإذا لم يؤدّ في الجزء الأول ينتقل السببية إلى آخر الجزء الممكن فيه الأداء) كما أن المسبب (الأداء) ينتقل بانتقال السبب إلى الثاني والثالث، أي يقع الأداء بالجزء الآخر الذي أراد المكلف الأداء فيه .
٣ - والثالث مذهب زفر وعند زفر (ينتقل السببية ) الى ما (جزء) يسع فيه، وبعد الخروج (خروج الوقت كله فالكل فكل الوقت متعين للسببية، بمعنى إذا مضى السبب بكامله ، ولم يفعل فيه شيئاً فيلزم عليه القضاء، حتى إذا أبلغ الصبي طهرت المرأة في آخر جزء من الوقت، ولم يسع الأداء فيه وجب عليها قضاء فرض ذلك الوقت .
٤ - والرابع مذهب أبي اليسر وروى عن أبي اليسر فخر الإسلام البزدوي (إمام الفقه والأصول) أن (الجزء) الأخير متعين للسببية حين خرج الوقت و بقى الجزء الآخر منه، فتنتقل السببية إلى الجزء الآخر.

ذكر دليل عامة الحنفية ثم الرد عليه : أما الدليل فقد استدل على مذهب الحنفية) بالاجماع على الوجوب (وجوب الصلاة) على من أسلم أو بلغ في وسط الوقت، والحاصل أن الشافعية والحنفية كلّهم أجمعوا على وجوب الصلاة على من صار مكلفاً في وسط الوقت، بأن أسلم الكافر أو بلغ فيه الضبي يجب عليهما الأداء، وإلا فعليهما القضاء، فعلم أن الوقت هو الأول، ولكن موسعاً ومنتقلاً إلى الثاني والثالث، وهكذا إلى بقاء الوقت، ولو كان الوقت في الواجب الموسع) هو الأول عيناً لم تجب الصلاة على من أسلم أو بلغ في وسط الوقت وليس الأمر كذلك، فثبت أن الوقت هو الأول ولكن موسعاً .

ثم قال في ردّ ذلك الاستدلال : ويمكن أن يقال إنه (وسط الوقت) وقت أول في حق من أسلم ومن بلغ في وسط الوقت أي كونه أول الوقت مخصوص بمن صار مكلفاً في وسط الوقت دون غيره، وهذا ردّ من جانب الشوافع على الحنفية بأن الوقت والسبب هو الأول عينا، دون موسعاً، والوسط أول في حق من صار مكلفاً فيه وقوله : ( فتدبر) إشارة إلى تفصيل الأول فإن المراد من الجزء الأول في حق من كان مكلفاً من السابق هو الأول الحقيقى، وفي حق من صار مكلفاً في الوسط أو في الأخير هو الاضافي، فكل وقت صار فيه المرء مكلفاً هو أول الوقت بالنسبة إليه فإنّ الخطاب توجّه إليه في ذلك الوقت .
The issue of causality in the time of the expanded obligation. The cause in the expanded [obligation] is the first part specifically according to the Shafi'is because of precedence, and according to the majority of Hanafis, rather it is extended to the last [part] like the effect, and according to Zufar to what accommodates performance, and after the exit then all [of the time]. And it is narrated from Abu al-Yusr that the last [part] is specific at that time. And he argued by consensus on the obligation upon one who embraced Islam or reached puberty in the middle of the time. And it can be said that it is the first for both of them, so reflect. The author mentioned in this issue four schools: 1) The first is the Shafi'i school: The cause in the expanded obligation is the first part specifically according to the Shafi'is because of precedence. 2) The second is the Hanafi school: According to the majority of Hanafis, rather the cause is the first part while being extended to the other part, so if one does not perform in the first part, causality transfers to the last part possible for performance, just as the effect (performance) transfers with the transfer of the cause to the second and third. 3) The third is the school of Zufar: According to Zufar, causality transfers to a part that accommodates it, and after the exit (exit of all the time) then all [of the time] is determined for causality, meaning that if the entire cause passes and he did nothing therein, then making up becomes obligatory. 4) The fourth is the school of Abu al-Yusr: It is narrated from Abu al-Yusr Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi, the imam of fiqh and usul, that the last part is determined for causality when the time exits and the other part remains, so causality transfers to the other part. The evidence of the majority of Hanafis is mentioned then the rebuttal: As for the evidence, they argued for the Hanafi school by consensus on the obligation (obligation of prayer) upon one who embraced Islam or reached puberty in the middle of the time. The summary is that the Shafi'is and Hanafis all agreed on the obligation of prayer upon one who becomes mukallaf in the middle of the time, such that if a disbeliever embraced Islam or a minor reached puberty therein, they must perform, otherwise they must make up. So it is known that the time is the first but extended and transferring to the second and third, and so on until the remaining time. And if the time in the expanded obligation were the first specifically, prayer would not be obligatory upon one who embraced Islam or reached puberty in the middle of the time, and this is not the case. So it is established that the time is the first but extended. Then he said in rebuttal of that argument: It can be said that it (middle of time) is a first time with respect to one who embraced Islam and one who reached puberty in the middle of the time, i.e., its being the first time is specific to one who becomes mukallaf in the middle of the time, not others. This is a rebuttal from the Shafi'i side against the Hanafis that the time and the cause is the first specifically, not extended, and the middle is first for the one who becomes mukallaf therein. And his saying: 'so reflect' is an indication of the detail of the first: that what is meant by the first part regarding one who was mukallaf from the beginning is the real first, and regarding one who becomes mukallaf in the middle or at the end is the relative first. So every time in which a person becomes mukallaf is the first time relative to him, because the address is directed to him at that time.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a dispute (ikhtilaf) among madhahib regarding the exact nature of the cause (sabab) that triggers the obligation in an expanded wajib (wajib mussa'), such as the five daily prayers whose time is broad. The author presents four positions. The Shafi'i position: the cause is the very first moment of the time, because it precedes all other moments. The majority Hanafi position (of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad): the cause is also the first moment, but it is extended (musa') and transfers to later moments if the act is not performed in the first; thus, the cause moves to the second moment, then the third, etc., just as the performance itself can be in any part. Zufar's position: the cause transfers to a part that is sufficient for the performance (i.e., the minimal time needed for the act). Abu al-Yusr al-Bazdawi's position: when the time is about to expire, the cause becomes fixed to the last part. The Hanafi majority argue by consensus: if a person becomes mukallaf (e.g., a disbeliever converts or a child reaches puberty) in the middle of the prayer time, both Shafi'is and Hanafis agree that the prayer is obligatory immediately, proving that the cause is the first moment for that person, but it is extended because someone who was already mukallaf could have performed earlier. The Shafi'i rebuttal is that for that person, the middle moment is effectively their 'first' time, not the real first. The author ends with 'so reflect' (fata dabbar), indicating that the resolution lies in distinguishing between the real first (for those continuously obligated) and the relative first (for those newly obligated). This is a classic usul dispute about the ontological status of time in expanded obligations.

Key Terms
السبب (as-sabab): The cause that triggers the obligation of an act; in expanded obligations, it is the first part of the time but extended.
الواجب الموسع (al-wajib al-mussa'): An obligatory act whose time is extensive, such as the five daily prayers; the obligation is fulfilled by performing it at any point within the time.
السببية (as-sababiyya): The quality of being the cause; the causality that attaches to a part of time for an expanded obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafis, what is the cause (sabab) of an expanded obligation like the five daily prayers?
A) The first part of the time, but extended and transferable to later parts.
B) The last part of the time, after which the time exits.
C) The entire time equally without specification.
D) Any part of the time that accommodates the performance.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi view that the cause of an expanded obligation is the first part but extended resolves the obligation for those who become mukallaf mid-time, but the Shafi'i alternative using relative first offers a competing interpretation.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

The Obligation of Completing Qada of Asr and the Principle of Ghalaba
Resolving an objection about qada of Asr using ghalaba and accidental deficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of ghalaba (majority) to determine the completeness of time for qada, and evaluate the objection regarding qada in a deficient time for a newly obligated person.

Core Idea
Time for qada is considered complete by its majority; deficiency due to resemblance to devil worshippers only affects ada, not qada.

This passage addresses an objection about making up the Asr prayer in a deficient time. The author first shifts from a proof based on partial deficiency to a proof that the whole time for qada is complete by the principle of ghalaba (majority). However, an objection arises: what if a person becomes obligated only at the end of a deficient time? Two answers are given: the first appeals to lack of contrary transmission; the second, more precise, explains that deficiency is accidental (due to resemblance to devil worshippers) and not intrinsic, so for qada one must choose a time free from such resemblance. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the shift in proof
The author abandons the initial proof that part of yesterday's Asr time is deficient and instead adopts a new proof: the entire time for qada (from first to last part) is complete because most of its parts are complete, and the majority gives its ruling to the whole.
This shift is triggered by an objection to the first proof.
2
Understand the objection to the new proof
The objection states: if a person becomes Muslim (or reaches puberty, or a woman becomes pure) only at the end of a deficient time and does not pray then, can he make it up in another deficient time on a different day? The objection argues that causality cannot be attributed to the whole time because the person was not obligated earlier.
The objection targets the assumption that the whole time is relevant for a person with partial obligation.
3
Analyze the first response
The first response simply denies the lack of validity: there is no transmission from early imams forbidding qada in a deficient time, so it is valid. This response is pragmatic but not principled.
The author describes this response as 'necessary' but not the best.
4
Analyze the second response (correct)
The second, more precise response explains that deficiency in time is not intrinsic (it is set for worship of Allah), but accidental, arising from the need to avoid resembling devil worshippers. For performance (ada), this deficiency is tolerated to honor the specific time. For qada, since one is bypassing the specific time, one must choose a complete time free from such resemblance.
This response uses the concept of 'mushabahat 'abadi al-shaytan' (resemblance to devil worshippers) as the rationale.
5
Derive the ruling and its rationale
The final ruling: it is not permissible to perform qada of Asr in a deficient time. The rationale is that qada must avoid any resemblance to devil worship, which would occur if performed at a time associated with their worship (ends of times).
This applies to any person who misses the prayer, not just one newly obligated.
