Combining Obligation and Prohibition: Genus, Species, and Aspect
Explains Hanafi position on combining wajib and haram in same act

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction between al-wāḥid bi al-jins and al-wāḥid bi al-nawʿ to determine when obligation and prohibition can combine in a single act, and evaluate the Mu'tazila objection.

Core Idea
Obligation and prohibition can coexist in a single act when the act has multiple species or multiple aspects — the key is whether the aspect (jiha) is unified or multiple.

This passage from Musallam al-Thubut tackles a foundational usul question: can an act be both wajib and haram at the same time? The Hanafi school answers with a nuanced distinction: in the same genus but different species, absolutely; in the same species, only if the aspects differ. We will walk through the reasoning and the rebuttals to the Mu'tazila.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish al-wāḥid bi al-jins and al-wāḥid bi al-nawʿ
Understand the two levels of unity: a single act considered as a genus (e.g., sujud) includes multiple species, while a single act as a species (e.g., a specific sujud) is a particular instance.
This distinction is the cornerstone of the Hanafi position.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila objection
The Mu'tazila argue that good and evil are intrinsic; thus one act cannot be both wajib and haram. They deny even the genus-level combination.
Their denial is called 'mukabara' (stubbornness) by the author.
3
Examine the Hanafi rebuttal using the prostration example
Prostration (sujud) as a genus includes wajib (to Allah) and haram (to sun). This shows combination in different species, not the same essence.
The Mu'tazila attempt to shift the combination to intention, but that also fails because intention is a universal.
4
Analyze the case of prayer in a usurped house
Here the act is one species (prayer) but has two aspects: being from prayer and being from usurpation. Since aspects differ, wajib and haram combine.
The sewing/traveling analogy reinforces that multiple aspects allow simultaneous obedience and disobedience.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafi says prayer in usurped house is valid (obligation drops) though sinful; other schools may invalidate it.
This affects whether the person must repeat the prayer or repent.

Issue of combining obligation and prohibition in one genus or one species. It is permissible in the one by genus for obligation and prohibition to combine, such as prostration to Allah Almighty and to the sun. Some Mu'tazila denied it out of obstinacy, and their turning it to the intention of glorification does not help. The discussion is about the one by species: either the aspect is unified in reality or in ruling, like when they are equal, then that is impossible, in fact it is an impossible obligation; or it is multiple, like prayer in the usurped house. According to the majority it is valid, and al-Qadi said it is not valid and the demand does not drop. Imam al-Razi considered it unlikely, and according to Ahmad and most theologians and al-Jubba'i it is not valid and does not drop. For us, the two objects are not unified in reality, for the being in the place, although it is one being in person, it is multiple in consideration: it is a being from the aspect that it is prayer and a being from the aspect that it is usurpation. It was said: The prohibition on being in the usurped place indicates that the being required in the command for prayer is other than it. I say: The indication is denied, for it is a branch of contradiction, and if we permit combination considering that the command is absolute as is its reality, then where is the indication? So it becomes like one who ordered his slave to sew and forbade him from traveling, and he sewed and traveled: he is obedient and disobedient definitely. The objection by fasting on the day of Nahr is repelled by saying that non-compliance is denied, for in our view one is released from responsibility by fasting on it. Even if granted, it is because of a preventer, which is the prohibition that indicates the invalidity of fasting on it, unlike the prohibition on usurpation, for it does not indicate the invalidity of prayer. And the answer by restricting the claim to what has an 'umum min wajh (general in one aspect) does not repel the objection from the generality of the evidence, unless it is said that the absolute general has no reality in the result except the reality of the specific, because of the unity of the ruling, so it would necessitate the combination of good and evil in the resulting reality. And in the general from one aspect there are two realities, so reflect. Also for us, if it were not valid, no disliked prayer would be established, because the rulings are contradictory, and the being is one. For the disliked is indeed the act, and if the dislikeness is due to a description, there is no difference between the prohibition of prohibition and the prohibition of dissuasion, so ponder. And he argued (that) if it were not valid, the obligation would not drop. Al-Qadi said it has dropped by consensus, but it was refuted by denying the existence of consensus, for if it had been, Ahmad (may Allah have mercy on him) would have known it. Then claiming two aspects of relief and usurpation in exiting from it, they attach to it from the error of Abu Hashim. How? It would necessitate the impossible obligation, rather the impossible obligation and the continuation of sin until one finishes as a deterrent, as Imam al-Haramayn went to, is not far. And the truth is that repentance wipes it out. This one: (1-1) It is permissible in a single act by genus that has different species for obligation and prohibition to combine, like prostration: it has types: prostration to Allah, prostration to the sun, prostration to the moon, and prostration to idols. So prostration to Allah is obligatory, and prostration to the sun and other false gods is forbidden. So obligation and prohibition combine in the types of a single genus. Some Mu'tazila denied their combination in a single act by genus like the mentioned prostration. Their denial is obstinacy and denial of the obvious. Their evidence is that good and evil for them are intrinsic in acts: the rational good necessitates the obligation of the act intrinsically, and its evil necessitates the prohibition of the act intrinsically. So obligation and prohibition do not combine in one essence. The author rebutted them and said: Their denial is obstinacy, because their combination is in two different types of one genus, not in one essence. And when the Mu'tazila said: The combination of obligation and prohibition is not in the act of prostration but in its necessary concomitant, which is the intention of glorification, so it is permissible for obligation and prohibition to combine in the intention of glorification: prostration with the intention of glorifying Allah is obligatory, and with the intention of glorifying other than Him is forbidden. The author said: Their turning the combination of obligation and prohibition to the intention of glorification instead of prostration does not benefit them nor help them in escaping from their combination in one genus by consideration
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is a usul al-fiqh dispute concerning the possibility of simultaneous obligation (wajib) and prohibition (haram) in a single act. The author, commenting on Musallam al-Thubut, first distinguishes between two levels: a single act in terms of genus (al-wahid bi al-jins) and a single act in terms of species (al-wahid bi al-naw'). Regarding the same genus, he argues that it is clearly possible for wajib and haram to combine; for example, prostration (sujud) as a genus includes both the obligatory prostration to Allah and the forbidden prostration to the sun or an idol. The Mu'tazila object, claiming that good and evil are intrinsic, so the same act cannot be both wajib and haram. The author dismisses this as stubbornness (mukabara) and notes that their move to attribute the combination to the intention (qasd al-ta'dhim) rather than the act itself does not resolve the issue because the genus remains one. As for the same species (al-wahid bi al-naw'), the key is the aspect (jiha). If the aspect is unified (e.g., the act is wajib and haram from exactly the same perspective), it is impossible. But if the aspect is multiple, as in the famous case of prayer in a usurped house (al-salat fi al-dar al-maghsuba), the Hanafi majority (jumhur) hold that the prayer is valid and the obligation drops, because the prohibition on being in the usurped location is separate from the prayer's commanded being; they are two different considerations (being from prayer and being from usurpation). The author provides an analogy: a master orders his slave to sew but forbids him from traveling; if the slave does both, he is simultaneously obedient and disobedient. The objection using fasting on the day of Nahr (Eid al-Adha) is countered: that prohibition directly invalidates the fast, but usurpation does not invalidate prayer. The passage also refutes al-Qadi's claim that the obligation does not drop, citing that the obligation manifestly does drop (since the person prayed) and that repentance suffices for the sin. The author also argues that if such combination were impossible, there would be no such thing as a makruh prayer; and the Hanafi school, unlike the Mu'tazila, holds that the rulings of wajib and haram are not inherently contradictory in one subject when the aspects differ. This detailed analysis reinforces the Hanafi position on the compatibility of wajib and haram under specific conditions, a key point in Hanafi usul.

Key Terms
الواحد بالجنس (al-wāḥid bi al-jins): A single act considered as a genus that includes multiple species, e.g., sujud as a category.
الواحد بالنوع (al-wāḥid bi al-nawʿ): A single act of a specific species; if the aspect (jiha) is multiple, wajib and haram can combine.
الجهة (al-jiha): The aspect or perspective from which the act is considered; e.g., being from prayer vs. being from usurpation.
مكابرة (mukābara): Stubborn denial of the obvious; used to describe Mu'tazila rejection of the clear example.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, which of the following is an example of combining wajib and haram in one act by genus?
A) Prayer in a usurped house
B) Fasting on the day of Eid al-Adha
C) Prostration to Allah and prostration to the sun
D) Obeying a master's command to sew and his prohibition to travel

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits the combination of obligation and prohibition in a single act when the act has multiple species (bi al-jins) or multiple aspects (bi al-naw' with different jiha).

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
12 min
Combining Obligation and Prohibition: Prayer in a Usurped House
Analyzing whether command and prohibition can coexist in one act, focusing on the single-type dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi concept of 'jihah' to explain the validity of prayer in a usurped house and evaluate the positions of the jumhur, al-Baqillani, al-Razi, and Ahmad.

Core Idea
Different aspects (jihaat) allow obligation and prohibition to coexist in the same act without contradiction.

This passage from a commentary on Musallam al-Thubut tackles a classic usul problem: can a single action be simultaneously obligatory and forbidden? The author first establishes that this is obvious across different species (e.g., prostration to God vs. to idols), then narrows the focus to a single type with different aspects—the famous case of prayer in a usurped house. Understanding how the Hanafi school resolves this using the concept of jihah is crucial for assessing rulings where command and prohibition overlap.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two levels of discussion
Notice the author first discusses combination in different species of one genus (sujud), then moves to combination in a single type (prayer in usurped house). Identify which level the dispute is about.
The Mu'tazila objection at the genus level is dismissed as obstinate.
2
Analyze the three states of jihah
The passage lists three states: (1) aspect of obligation is identical to prohibition, (2) aspects are different but equal (virtually unified), (3) aspects are different. Understand why the first two make combination impossible, while the third is the subject of dispute.
Prayer in usurped house falls under the third state.
3
Identify the four madhhabs on prayer in usurped house
The passage presents four positions: (a) jumhur (including Hanafis): valid but sinful, no reward; (b) al-Baqillani: invalid but no repeat required; (c) al-Razi: rejects al-Baqillani's view; (d) Ahmad and most mutakallimun: likely invalid with repeat (text incomplete).
The Hanafi position is the first one.
4
Trace the reasoning behind each position
For each madhhab, ask: what is the key usul principle? Hanafis use jihah (different aspects) to separate obligation from prohibition; al-Baqillani sees the prohibition as corrupting the act; al-Razi insists that invalidity logically implies the demand remains.
Al-Razi's critique of al-Baqillani highlights a logical inconsistency.
5
Apply the Hanafi jihah doctrine to a new scenario
Take a scenario like offering a supererogatory prayer in a stolen garment. Use the same jihah analysis: the obligation (sunnah) is from the command, the prohibition from the theft. Conclude: prayer is valid but sinful, no reward for the innovation of committing theft.
This demonstrates the practical utility of the principle.

1. It is permissible in a single action of a genus that has different species for obligation and prohibition to combine, like prostration (sujud) for it has species: prostration to God and prostration to the sun, and prostration to the moon, and prostration to idols. So prostration to God is obligatory, and prostration to the sun and other false deities is forbidden. Thus obligation and prohibition combine in the species of a single genus. Some Mu'tazila denied that they combine in a single action of a genus like the mentioned prostration. Their denial is obstinate and a denial of the obvious. Their proof is that according to them, good and evil are intrinsic to actions: intellectual good necessitates the obligation of the action essentially, and its evil necessitates the prohibition of the action essentially. So obligation and prohibition cannot combine in a single essence. The author refuted them, saying: Their denial is obstinate because their combination is in two different species of one genus, not in a single essence. When the Mu'tazila said that the combination of obligation and prohibition is not in the action of prostration but in its necessary concomitant which is the intention of glorification, so it is permissible for obligation and prohibition to combine in the intention of glorification: prostration with the intention of glorifying God is obligatory, and with the intention of glorifying other than God is forbidden. The author said: Their shifting of the combination of obligation and prohibition to the intention of glorification instead of prostration does not avail them nor benefit them in escaping from their combination in one genus with respect to its two species, for the intention of glorification is also a genus that has species: it is permissible for them to combine in the intention of glorification with respect to its two species – the intention of glorifying God and the intention of glorifying other than God. And the specification of disallowance to actions of the limbs is meaningless, i.e., specifying the disallowance of combination to actions of the limbs and allowing it in actions of the heart. The discussion and dispute is about the permissibility of combining obligation and prohibition in a single action of a type (naw') under which fall different individuals. That single type is not free from three states: 1. Either the aspect (jihah) of obligation and prohibition are truly unified, such that the aspect of obligation is the aspect of prohibition. 2. Or the aspect is unified virtually (hukman), such that the aspect of obligation is different from the aspect of prohibition but they are equal, so they become like a single aspect virtually. In these two states, combining obligation and prohibition is impossible because it entails the combination of two contradictories; rather, the obligation of such a combination and that single type action is impossible due to what was mentioned. Or those aspects are different, like the act of prayer in a usurped house, for the obligation is from the aspect that it is commanded, and the prohibition is from the aspect that it is performed in the property of another without his consent. Such a single type is the subject of dispute. There are four madhhabs regarding it: Determination of the subject of dispute: 1. The first madhhab is that of the majority (jumhur): According to the majority, the prayer is valid, even though that act is forbidden, and there is no reward for the worshiper. 2. The second madhhab is that of Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani: He said the prayer is not valid, but the demand (talab) is removed, i.e., the demand for making it up again is removed from the responsible person. 3. The third madhhab is that of Imam al-Razi, and he (i.e., the author?) considered it far-fetched: Imam al-Razi (in al-Mahsool) considered it far-fetched because lack of validity contradicts the removal of the demand. 4. The fourth madhhab is that of Ahmad and most of the mutakallimun and al-Jubba'i: According to Ahmad and most mutakallimun...

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Musallam al-Thubut addresses the classic usul al-fiqh issue of whether obligation (wujub) and prohibition (hurmah) can combine in a single act. The author distinguishes two levels: (1) Combining across different species of one genus – e.g., prostration (sujud) as a genus has types: prostration to God (obligatory), prostration to the sun (forbidden). This is unproblematic and the Mu'tazila who deny it are accused of obstinacy. (2) Combining in a single act of the same type (naw' wahid) – e.g., prayer in a usurped house. Here, the author identifies three states based on the aspects (jihaat) of the act. If the aspects are truly unified or virtually unified (equal), combination is impossible. But if aspects are different – prayer has the aspect of being commanded (wajib) and the aspect of involving usurpation (haram) – then there is dispute. The passage then presents four madhhabs: (i) Jumhur (including Hanafis): prayer valid but sinful, no reward. (ii) al-Baqillani: invalid but no requirement to repeat. (iii) al-Razi: considered far-fetched because invalidity implies the demand is not removed. (iv) Ahmad and most mutakallimun: their view not fully stated due to page cut. The Hanafi position aligns with the jumhur: prayer is valid in its essence (because the command for prayer is absolute) but the act is sinful due to the prohibition against usurpation. This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
الوجوب (al-wujub): The legal obligation to perform an act; the act becomes wajib.
الحرمة (al-hurmah): Legal prohibition; the act is haram and sinful.
الفعل الواحد بالنوع (al-fi'l al-wahid bi al-naw'): A single action considered as a type (species) under which multiple individual instances fall, e.g., prayer as a type.
الجهة (al-jihah): The aspect or perspective from which a ruling applies to an act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority (jumhur), including the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on prayer performed in a usurped house?
A) The prayer is invalid and must be repeated.
B) The prayer is valid but sinful, and no reward is given.
C) The prayer is invalid but the obligation is removed.
D) The prayer is valid and reward is given.

Key Takeaway
Prayer in a usurped house is valid but sinful according to the Hanafi school, because the obligation and prohibition apply through different aspects (jihah).

Combining Obligation and Prohibition in One Act: The Usul Dispute
Explains the issue of combining obligation and prohibition in one action, with four madhāhib and proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four madhāhib on combining wujūb and ḥurmah in one act by type, and apply the Hanafi proof of taʿaddud al-jihāt to the case of ṣalāh in a ghāṣb land.

Core Idea
When an act has two different aspects, obligation and prohibition can coexist without logical contradiction.

This lesson explores a classic usul al-fiqh debate: can an act be simultaneously obligatory and prohibited? The Hanafi school, following the majority, argues yes, provided the obligation and prohibition attach to different aspects of the same act. Using the case of prayer in a usurped property, we will examine four scholarly positions and the Hanafi proof based on taʿaddud al-jihāt.

Guided Path
1
Define the issue
Identify what 'al-wāḥid al-nawʿī' means and the three scenarios described: (1) aspects identical, (2) aspects legally identical, (3) aspects truly different. The dispute is only about the third scenario.
The first two scenarios are impossible; the third is possible.
2
List the four madhāhib
State each position from the text: majority (prayer valid, sinful, no reward), al-Bāqillānī (invalid, demand falls), al-Rāzī (rejects al-Bāqillānī's view), Aḥmad and mutakallimūn (invalid, demand remains).
The Hanafi school aligns with the majority.
3
Analyze the majority proof
Understand the proof: the two objects (mutʿallaq) are different – worship (ʿibādah) vs. usurpation (ghaṣb). The existence of the act is considered from two aspects, so no contradiction.
The key phrase: 'فإن وجود الصلاة... متعدد باعتبار...'
4
Respond to the objection
Examine the objection: the prohibition shows that the commanded 'being' is different from the prohibited 'being', so the commanded act is not performed. Then study the Hanafi answer: the indication (dalāla) is not conceded because the command is absolute.
The answer begins with 'أقول الدلالة ممنوعة'.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: according to the majority, the prayer is valid and no qaḍāʾ is required; according to Aḥmad and others, it is invalid and must be repeated.
This affects fatwa in cases of ṣalāh on usurped land.

The discussion and dispute is only about the permissibility of combining obligation and prohibition in one action by type, which has various individuals. That unit by type does not lack three states: 1. Either the aspect of obligation and prohibition are truly united, such that the aspect of obligation is the aspect of prohibition. 2. Or the aspect is united in effect, such that the aspect of obligation is other than the aspect of prohibition but the two aspects are equal, so they become like one aspect in effect. In these two states, combining obligation and prohibition is impossible because it necessitates combining opposites; indeed, the obligation of such a combination and that single action by type is impossible. 3. Or the aspect is unified, like the act of prayer in a usurped house, for the obligation is from the aspect that it is commanded, and the prohibition is from the aspect that it is performed in another's property without his consent. Such a single type is the subject of dispute, and there are four schools regarding it: 1. The first is the school of the majority: according to the majority, the prayer is valid although that act is prohibited, and there is no reward for the worshipper. 2. The second is the school of al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani: al-Qadi said the prayer is invalid and the demand (for performance) falls away, meaning the demand for repetition is lifted from the obligated person. 3. The third is the school of Imam al-Razi, and Imam al-Razi in al-Mahsul considered this opinion unlikely because invalidity contradicts the falling away of the demand. 4. The fourth is the school of Ahmad and most of the mutakallimun and al-Jubba'i: the prayer is invalid and the demand does not fall away. The proof of the majority for the permissibility of combining obligation and prohibition in the single type with multiple aspects, and for the validity of prayer in usurped land, is as follows: (The proof) for us is the true non-unity of the two objects (the object of obligation and the object of prohibition). That is, the aspect of obligation and the aspect of prohibition are truly different. So the object of obligation is the worship of God, and the object of prohibition is the use of another's property without his consent. He said concerning the proof of the true non-unity of the two objects: For the existence of prayer in the specific place, the usurped house, although it is one in person (specific act), it is multiple in that it is an existence from the aspect that it is prayer, and an existence from the aspect that it is usurpation. So the two existences are different from the aspect of worship of God and from the aspect of using another's property without his consent. Thus the objects of obligation and prohibition differ. This is the meaning of the author's statement: "Indeed, being in a place, although it is one for a person, is multiple in that it is a being from the aspect that it is prayer, and a being from the aspect that it is usurpation." As for the objection, it has been said: The prohibition of prayer being in the usurped place indicates that the being required in the command for prayer is other than the being of prayer in the usurped place, meaning the existence required in the command for prayer is other than the existence of prayer in the usurped place, so one has not performed the commanded act. As for the answer, it begins from his saying: I say the indication is not conceded, for it is a branch of contradiction, and if we permit the combination (of obligation and prohibition) considering that the command is absolute, as it is in reality, then where is the indication? The summary of the answer: We do not concede that the prohibition of existence in a usurped house indicates that
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is an extended discussion of a classic usul al-fiqh issue: whether wujūb (obligation) and hurmah (prohibition) can combine in a single act that is one in type (nawʿ) but has multiple aspects (jihāt). The author, an Hanafi commentator, explains that the unit under discussion is the single type (al-wāḥid al-nawʿī), which covers various individual acts. He first delineates three possible scenarios: (1) the aspect of obligation and prohibition are truly identical, (2) they are legally identical due to equivalence, and (3) they are truly different. In the first two cases, combination is impossible because it would entail contradictory obligations (naqīḍayn). The third case—where the aspects differ, such as performing prayer (obligatory) in a usurped space (prohibited)—is the locus of dispute. Then the author presents four madhāhib: (i) the majority (al-jumhūr) hold that the act (prayer) is valid but sinful with no reward; (ii) al-Bāqillānī says it is invalid but the obligation to repeat (qaḍāʾ) falls; (iii) al-Rāzī rejects the latter because invalidity cannot coexist with falling of the demand; (iv) Aḥmad b. Ḥanbal and most mutakallimūn, including al-Jubbāʾī, say it is invalid and the demand remains. The author then provides the majority's proof: the two objects (mutʿallaq) are truly different—the object of obligation is worship of Allah, while the object of prohibition is using another's property without consent. Even though the specific act is one, its existence as prayer and as usurpation are distinct aspects, so the two rulings apply to different aspects. An objection arises: the prohibition of being in the usurped place implies that the commanded 'being' (kawn) is different from the prohibited 'being', thus the commanded act is not fulfilled. The answer begins by denying that the prohibition indicates this, because the command is absolute and we allow combination; thus the indication (dalāla) is not conceded. The answer is cut off but continues the argument.

Key Terms
الواحد النوعي (al-wāḥid al-nawʿī): A single type of act that encompasses multiple individual instances (asḥkāṣ). It is the category under which specific acts fall.
جهة الوجوب والحرمة (jihah al-wujūb wa al-ḥurmah): The aspect or perspective from which an act is considered obligatory or prohibited.
متعلق الوجوب والحرمة (mutʿallaq al-wujūb wa al-ḥurmah): The object to which the obligation or prohibition is attached. In the prayer in usurped land example, the object of obligation is the worship of Allah, and the object of prohibition is the use of another's property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority (including Hanafis), what is the ruling on performing salah in a usurped land?
A) The salah is invalid and must be repeated.
B) The salah is valid but sinful and has no reward.
C) The salah is valid and rewarded.
D) The salah is invalid but no repetition is required.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school resolves the apparent contradiction between obligation and prohibition in one act by distinguishing the aspects, so the prayer in a usurped place is valid though sinful.

Combining Obligation and Prohibition: Prayer in Usurped Land
Hanafi defense of combination of rulings via distinct aspects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that prayer in usurped land is valid while the opposing school rejects it, and apply the concept of 'al-wāḥid al-naw‘ī muta‘addid al-jihah' to distinguish the objects of obligation and prohibition.

Core Idea
A single act can be both obligatory and prohibited when viewed from different aspects, because the objects of the rulings differ.

This passage tackles a classic usul dispute: can an act be simultaneously commanded and forbidden? The Hanafi school says yes, by analyzing the act under distinct legal aspects. The key is that the obligation relates to the worship aspect, while the prohibition relates to the trespass aspect. Understanding this principle resolves apparent contradictions in rulings and allows for valid yet sinful acts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Contested Issue
Determine the exact point of ikhtilaf: Is prayer in usurped land valid or invalid? The Hanafi position is that it is valid but sinful, while the opposing school says it is invalid.
The dispute is not about the sinfulness (both agree it is sinful) but about the validity of the prayer as a commanded act.
2
Examine the Hanafi Evidence
Focus on the argument from non-unity of the objects (mutā‘allaq). The object of obligation is worship of Allah; the object of prohibition is using another's property without consent. The prayer's existence is one in person but multiple in aspect (as worship and as trespass).
The Arabic phrase 'وجود من حيث أنه صلاة، ووجود من حيث أنه غصب' captures the duality.
3
Analyze the Objection and Response
The opponent claims that the prohibition on the location indicates the commanded existence (prayer) must be different from the forbidden existence (prayer in that place). The Hanafi response: the command is absolute (mutlaq) and not restricted to a location; the prohibition only creates otherness if there is direct contradiction, which there is not.
The response hinges on the absoluteness of the command and the lack of contradiction (tadād) between the two aspects.
4
Apply the Slave Analogy
The author analogizes to a slave commanded to sew but forbidden from using a specific thread. If the slave sews while traveling (using a different thread), he is both obedient and disobedient. Similarly, the praying person is obedient in performing prayer and disobedient in the location.
The analogy illustrates that a single act can be evaluated under two legal categories without contradiction.
5
Test Consistency with the Counterexample
The opponent raises the case of fasting on the day of Nahr (vowed fast) – if the same logic applies, it should also be valid. The Hanafi response: we accept that it is valid; according to the Hanafi school, a vowed fast on that day is valid but sinful, consistent with the principle.
This shows the Hanafi position is internally consistent.

دليل الجمهور على جواز الاجتماع في الواحد النوعي متعدد الجهة ودليل الجمهور على جواز اجتماع الوجوب والحرمة في الواحد النوعي متعدد الجهة، وعلى صحة الصلاة في الأرض المغصوبة ما يأتي: (الدليل) لنا عدم اتحاد المتعلقين (متعلَّق الوجوب ومتعلق الحرمة) حقيقة. أي جهة الوجوب وجهة الحرمة مختلفتان حقيقة، فمتعلّق الوجوب هو عبادة الله ومتعلق الحرمة استعمال ملك الغير بلا رضاه. وقال في دليل عدم اتحاد المتعلقين حقيقة: فإن وجود الصلاة في المكان الخاص الدار المغصوبة (وإن كان واحداً بالشخص، لكنه متعدد باعتبار أنه وجود من حيث أنه صلاة، ووجود من حيث أنه غصب، فالوجودان مختلفان من حيث عبادة الله، ومن حيث استعمال ملك الغير بدون رضاه، فاختلف متعلّق الوجوب والحرمة. وهذا مفهوم قول المصنف: "فإن الكؤنَ في الحيز وإن كان واحداً لشخص لكنه متعدد باعتبار أنه كون من حيث أنه صلاة، وكون من حيث أنه غصب". الإشكال على صحة الصلاة في الأرض المغصوبة والجواب عنه أما الإشكال فقد قيل: (إنّ) النهي عن كون الصلاة في المكان المغصوب يدل على أن الكون (الوجود) المطلوب في الأمر بالصلاة غيره، (غير كون الصلاة في المكان المغصوب)، يعني الوجود المطلوب في الأمر بالصلاة غير وجود الصلاة في المكان المغصوب، فلم يحصل الإتيان بالمأمور به. وأنا الجواب فهو يبدأ من قوله: أقول الدلالة ممنوعة؛ فإنها فرع التضاد، وإذا جوزنا الاجتماع (اجتماع الوجوب والحرمة) نظراً إلى أن الأمر مطلق، كما هو حقيقته، فأين الدلالة؟ وحاصل الجواب: أنا لانسلّم أن النهي عن الوجود في دار مغصوبة يدل على أن الوجود المطلوب بالأمر بالصلاة غير الوجود في الدار المغصوبة؛ لأن الأمر بالصلاة ووجودها عام من أن يكون في دار مغصوبة أو في غيرها، إذا الأمر بالضلاة وإيجادها مطلق عن قيد كونه في دار ما (مملوكة كانت أو مغصوبة)، وإنما يدل النهي على غيرية الوجودين إذا كان بينهما تضاد، ولا تضاد بين وجود الصلاة وبين النهي عن الوجود في دار مغصوبة ولا دلالة على المغايرة والمنافاة وهذا معنى قوله: فأين الدلالة؟ بل بينهما (بين الوجود في الدار المغصوبة وبين وجود الصلاة) عموم وخصوص من وجه. فالأمر (الأمر بالصلاة) باقي على إطلاقه كما هو حقيقته. فجاز أن تكون الدار لشئ آخر، ولا تكون فيها الصلاة، وجاز أن توجد الصلاة في دار غير المغصوبة كما هو الأصل. (۱) وهذا معنى قوله: فأين الدلالة؟ فصار أداء الصلاة في دار مغصوبة، كما إذا أمر عبده بالخياطة ونهاه عن الشفر، فخاط (أي جاء بالمأمور به) وسافر، فإنه (العبد) مطيع وعاص قطعاً. كذلك في أداء الصلاة في الدار المغصوبة فالمصلي مطيع بأداء الصلاة، وعاص بأدائها في دار مغصوبة. نقض صحة الصلاة في دار مغصوبة بعدم صحة صوم يوم النحر تفصيل النقض: أنه لو صحت الصلاة في دار مغصوبة لعدم اتحاد المتعلقين (متعلق الوجوب ومتعلق الحرمة) لكان صوم يوم النحر أيضا باعتبار الجهتين (جهة الوجوب المتعلق بالعبادة، وجهة الحرمة المتعلقة بالإعراض عن الضيافة، فكونه صوماً عبادة، وكونه في يوم النحر ممنوع، فهما أمران مختلفان، كالصلاة، والصلاة في الدار المغصوبة. فلا بد أن يصح صوم يوم النحر (المنذور فيه) ولا تصح الصلاة في الأرض المغصوبة فأجاب عنه المصنف بجوابين: أولهما منعي وثانيهما تسليمي (وهذا النقض) مدفوع بأن تخلف حكم الصلاة في الدار المغصوبة عن حكم صوم يوم النحر (نذراً) ممنوع، فإنا نلتزم صحة صوم يوم النحر كصحة الصلاة في الدار المغصوبة)، فعندنا (الحنفية) يخرج ناذر صوم يوم النحر
Evidence of the majority on the permissibility of combination in the single generic [act] with multiple aspects, and evidence of the majority on the permissibility of combining obligation and prohibition in the single generic [act] with multiple aspects, and on the validity of prayer in usurped land is as follows: (The evidence) for us is the non-unity of the two objects (object of obligation and object of prohibition) in reality. That is, the aspect of obligation and the aspect of prohibition are different in reality. So the object of obligation is worship of Allah, and the object of prohibition is using another's property without his consent. And he said in the proof of the non-unity of the two objects in reality: for the existence of prayer in the specific place, the usurped house, (even though it is one in person, yet it is multiple by consideration: it is an existence insofar as it is prayer, and an existence insofar as it is usurpation, so the two existences are different from the perspective of worship of Allah and from the perspective of using another's property without his consent. Thus the objects of obligation and prohibition differed. And this is the meaning of the author's statement: 'For the being in a location, even though it is one in person, yet it is multiple by consideration: it is a being insofar as it is prayer, and a being insofar as it is usurpation.') The objection against the validity of prayer in usurped land and the answer to it: As for the objection, it has been said: (Indeed) the prohibition of being of prayer in the usurped place indicates that the being (existence) required by the command to pray is other than the existence of prayer in the usurped place, meaning the existence required by the command to pray is other than the existence of prayer in the usurped place, so the commanded act has not been performed. And as for the answer, it begins from his statement: I say the indication is denied; for it is a branch of contradiction, and if we permit the combination (combination of obligation and prohibition) considering that the command is absolute, as is its reality, so where is the indication? The summary of the answer: We do not accept that the prohibition of existence in a usurped house indicates that the existence required by the command to pray is other than the existence in the usurped house; because the command to pray and its existence are general as to whether it is in a usurped house or in another, since the command to pray and its actualization are absolute without the restriction of it being in a house (whether owned or usurped). The prohibition only indicates otherness of the two existences if there is contradiction between them, and there is no contradiction between the existence of prayer and the prohibition of existence in a usurped house, and no indication of difference and conflict. And this is the meaning of his statement: 'So where is the indication?' Rather, between them (between the existence in the usurped house and the existence of prayer) there is general and specific from one perspective. The command (the command to pray) remains on its absoluteness as is its reality. So it is possible that the house is for something else and prayer is not in it, and it is possible that prayer exists in a non-usurped house as is the original. (1) And this is the meaning of his statement: 'So where is the indication?' So performing prayer in a usurped house becomes like when he commands his slave to sew and forbids him from the (sewing) thread, so he sewed (i.e., performed the commanded act) and traveled; then the slave is obedient and disobedient definitively. Similarly, in performing prayer in the usurped house, the praying person is obedient by performing the prayer and disobedient by performing it in a usurped house. Refutation of the validity of prayer in a usurped house by the invalidity of fasting on the day of Nahr. Detail of the refutation: If prayer in a usurped house were valid due to the non-unity of the two objects (object of obligation and object of prohibition), then fasting on the day of Nahr would also be valid by considering the two aspects (aspect of obligation related to worship, and aspect of prohibition related to turning away from hospitality; so it being fasting is worship, and it being on the day of Nahr is prohibited, so they are two different matters, like prayer and prayer in the usurped house). So fasting on the day of Nahr (vowed) must be valid, and prayer in usurped land must not be valid. So the author answered it with two answers: the first is a denial and the second is a concession. (And this refutation) is repelled because the difference in ruling of prayer in the usurped house from the ruling of fasting on the day of Nahr (vowed) is denied; for we commit to the validity of fasting on the day of Nahr like the validity of prayer in the usurped house. For according to us (the Hanafis), the one who vows to fast on the day of Nahr exits [from his vow].
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi usul disputation on the validity of prayer in usurped land. The author presents the majority (جمهور) evidence for combining obligation and prohibition in a single generic act with multiple aspects. The core Hanafi argument is that the object of obligation (worship of Allah) is distinct from the object of prohibition (using another's property without consent) even if the physical act is one. The existence of prayer in a specific location is considered under two aspects: as prayer (worship) and as usurpation (trespass). Thus, the two legal rulings apply to different aspects, so there is no contradiction. The author then addresses an objection: the prohibition on the location suggests the commanded existence (prayer) must be different from the forbidden existence (prayer in usurped place), so the commanded act is not achieved. The response is that the command is absolute and not restricted to a specific location; the prohibition only creates otherness if there is direct contradiction between the two existences, which there isn't. The prayer exists as a single act but is evaluated under two different legal categories. An analogy is given: a slave commanded to sew but forbidden from using a specific thread; if he sews while traveling (using a different thread?), he is both obedient and disobedient. Similarly, the prayer in usurped land is both valid (as prayer) and sinful (as trespass). Finally, a counterexample is raised: if this logic holds, fasting on the day of Nahr (prohibited) should also be valid if vowed. The Hanafi response: they do hold it valid; according to Hanafis, if someone vows to fast on the day of Nahr, the fast is valid but sinful. Thus, the principle is consistent.

Key Terms
اجتماع الوجوب والحرمة (ijtimā‘ al-wujūb wa al-ḥurmah): The coexistence of an obligation and a prohibition in a single act due to different aspects (جهات) to which the rulings attach.
الواحد النوعي متعدد الجهة (al-wāḥid al-naw‘ī muta‘addid al-jihah): A single generic act (e.g., a particular prayer) that can be considered under multiple aspects (e.g., as worship and as spatial occupation).
متعلق (muta‘allaq): The object of a legal ruling; in this context, the act or attribute to which the obligation or prohibition is directed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi evidence presented, why is prayer in a usurped land considered valid despite the prohibition?
A) Because the prohibition on usurped land is not absolute.
B) Because the obligation and prohibition relate to different objects: worship of Allah versus use of another's property.
C) Because the prayer is performed with the owner's implicit permission.
D) Because the command to pray abrogates the prohibition on usurpation.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates prayer in usurped land by distinguishing the objects of obligation and prohibition, using the concept of a single generic act with multiple legal aspects.

Mandub is Not a Taklif: A Usul Dispute
Explaining that the recommended act is not a legal obligation (taklif), contrary to the view of al-Isfara'ini.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that the recommended act (mandub) is not a legal charge (taklif) and evaluate al-Isfara'ini's opposing argument.

Core Idea
A recommended act is not a taklif because leaving it is not a sin.

This lesson examines a key usul al-fiqh dispute: is the recommended act (mandub) a form of legal obligation (taklif)? The Hanafi school maintains it is not, while the Shafi'i scholar Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini argued otherwise. By analyzing the arguments and proposed reconciliations, you will gain a deeper understanding of the category of taklif and its limits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position on mandub
The Hanafis hold that mandub is not a taklif because the agent has freedom to leave it without sin. This is based on the absence of blame.
2
Understand al-Isfara'ini's opposing argument
He considered mandub a taklif because one is charged to attain reward. This implies a broader conception of taklif.
3
Analyze the author's first interpretation
The author suggests al-Isfara'ini might have meant the obligation to believe in the recommendation, not the act itself. This interpretation is then criticized as conflating two separate rulings.
4
Examine the second reconciliation
Broadening the definition of taklif to include any shari'ah address (khitab) resolves the dispute terminologically. This shows how definitional choices affect the outcome.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المندوب ليس بتكليف (أي ليس مما كلف بعمله الإنسان) لأنه في سعة من تركه ، أي ليس تركه معصية، خلافاً للأستاذ (أبي إسحاق الإسفرائني) فإنه قال : هو مما يكلف به لتحصيل الثواب ، ثم قال المصنف في تأويل كلام الأستاذ: ولعله أراد وجوب اعتقاد الندبية، أي يُكلّف باعتقاد ندبيته وكونه مما يُحصل به الثواب ، ثم استشهد بالمباح وقال : ولهذا جُعِل المباح تكليفاً مما يكلف به الإنسان ، أي لأجل إرادة اعتقاد الإباحة جعل المباح تكليفاً وإلا في ترك المباح أيضاً سعة، فكيف يكون مما كلف به ؟ ولكن يرد على هذا التأويل أن الندب أو الإباحة حكم، وذلك أي وجوب اعتقاد الندبية والإباحة حكم آخر ، فكيف يجعلها الأستاذ حكماً واحداً، حتى يصح التأويل؟ وأشار المصنف إلى توجيه آخر وقال: ولو جعل نفس خطاب الشارع تكليفاً لم يبعد (أي لو جُعِل خطاب الله تعالى المتعلق بأفعال العباد اقتضاء أو تخييراً) مما كلف به لم يبعد؛ لأنه يشمل الوجوب والندب والإباحة والكراهة كلها بمعانيها المعتبرة ، سواء كان التكليف بها عقلاً (اعتقادا) أو فعلاً أو قولاً، ولا يبقى إشكال.
The recommended (mandub) is not a taklif (i.e., not something with which a person is charged to do) because one has leeway in leaving it, i.e., leaving it is not disobedience, contrary to the teacher (Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini). For he said: It is something with which one is charged to attain reward. Then the author said in interpreting the teacher's statement: Perhaps he intended the obligation of believing in its being recommended (wujub i'tiqad al-nadbiyyah), i.e., one is charged with believing in its being recommended and that it is something by which reward is attained. Then he cited the permissible (mubah) and said: And for this the permissible is made a taklif in that one is charged with it, i.e., for the sake of intending belief in its being permissible, the permissible is made a taklif; otherwise, in leaving the permissible there is also leeway, so how could it be something with which one is charged? But this interpretation is objected to: that the recommended or the permissible is a ruling, and that (i.e., the obligation of believing in its being recommended or permissible) is another ruling, so how can the teacher make them one ruling so that the interpretation is valid? Then the author pointed to another direction and said: And if one were to make the address of the Lawgiver itself a taklif, it would not be far-fetched (i.e., if one makes the address of Allah Almighty related to the actions of servants, whether as requirement or choice, something with which one is charged, it would not be far-fetched); because it includes obligation, recommendation, permission, and prohibition all with their considered meanings, whether the taklif with them is intellectual (belief) or actual or verbal, and no problem remains.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific usul question: is the recommended act (mandub) a type of taklif (legal charge)? The author affirms the Hanafi position: mandub is not a taklif because the agent has full freedom to leave it without penalty or sin. This is in opposition to Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini, a prominent Shafi'i usuli, who considered mandub a taklif because one is charged with it to attain reward. The author offers a possible interpretation of al-Isfara'ini's view: he might have meant that there is an obligation to believe that the act is recommended, not that the act itself is obligatory. However, this interpretation is criticized because the ruling of being recommended (nadb) is separate from the ruling of believing it is recommended. The author then suggests another reconciliation: if we define 'taklif' as any shari'ah address (khitab) to the actions of servants, whether requiring or allowing, then it would include all categories (wajib, mandub, mubah, etc.) and the dispute becomes terminological. This second interpretation resolves the issue by broadening the definition of taklif, but the author makes clear that in the strict sense, mandub is not a taklif.

Key Terms
التكليف (al-taklif): A legal charge that demands performance or avoidance, with consequence for violation.
المندوب (al-mandub): An act whose doer is praised and whose leaver is not blamed; not a taklif.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why is the recommended act (mandub) not considered a taklif?
A) Because leaving it is a sin.
B) Because one has the leeway to leave it without sin.
C) Because it is not addressed by the Lawgiver.
D) Because it is only a recommended act and has no legal status.

Key Takeaway
The recommended act (mandub) is not a legal charge (taklif) in the strict Hanafi sense because its omission incurs no sin, a defining feature of taklif.

Defining Legal Rulings: Is Ibaha a Hukm Shar'i?
Defending ibaha as a legal ruling against Mu'tazila

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of hukm shar'i and argue why ibaha is included, contrasting with the Mu'tazila position.

Core Idea
Ibaha fits the definition of hukm shar'i because option (takhyir) is legally addressed through the absence of restrictive evidence.

In this passage, we explore a foundational dispute in usul al-fiqh: whether permissibility (ibaha) qualifies as a legal ruling (hukm shar'i). The Hanafi school argues it does, citing the definition of hukm shar'i and the role of legal indicators (madarik). Some Mu'tazila object, claiming ibaha existed before revelation and carries no reward or punishment. By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Hanafi methodology for establishing ibaha as a legal ruling and how it responds to opposing views.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
Clearly state the question: Is ibaha (permissibility) a hukm shar'i? Note that the Hanafi position is affirmative, opposed by some Mu'tazila.
The issue is not about the ruling of ibaha itself, but its classification within the category of hukm shar'i.
2
Analyze the Hanafi definition of hukm shar'i
Recite the definition from the passage: 'the address of Allah related to the actions of the responsible person in terms of requirement (iqtida') or option (takhyir).' Understand that option is precisely ibaha.
Hanafis include both command/prohibition and permission under this definition.
3
Examine the Mu'tazila objection
Understand the opposing view: ibaha existed before revelation, so it cannot be a revealed ruling. Also, reward/punishment are not attached, unlike obligations.
The objection rests on a narrower definition of hukm shar'i.
4
Work through the Hanafi response
The passage explains that original ibaha (before the Law) is a type of legal ibaha because the absence of restrictive evidence (madrak shar'i) itself constitutes a legal indicator of the Lawgiver's ruling of option. General addresses (like 'Leave me with what I have left you') confirm this.
The Hanafi response relies on treating the lack of evidence as an evidence (al-'adam al-mudrak as madrak).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة كون الإباحة حكما شرعيا
الإباحة حكم شرعى؛ لأنه خطاب الشرع تخييراً، والإباحة الأصلية نوع منه، لأن كل ما عدم فيه المدرك الشرعي للحرج في فعله وتركه فذلک مدرك شرعى لحكم الشارع بالتخيير، فهي لا تكون إلا بعد الشرع، خلافاً لبعض المعتزلة وقد تقدم
الإباحة حكم شرعى ، لأنه خطاب الشرع تخييراً، والإباحة الأصلية (الإباحة قبل الشرع) نوع منه ، لأنّ كلّ ماعدم فيه المدرك الشرعي للحرج في فعله وتركه، فذلك مَدْرَك شرعى لحكم الشارع بالتخيير، فهي (الإباحة) لا تكون إلا بعد الشرع، خلافاً لبعض المعتزلة وقد تقدم .
و ذكر المصنف حول الإباحة أربع مسائل: هذه أولها:
(۱) ماهي الضرورة لذكر هذه المسألة: أن الإباحة حكم شرعى ؟
و تفصيل السؤال لاشك أن المبحوث عنه في هذا الباب هي الأحكام الشرعية والإباحة ليست منها ، لأنها كانت قبل الشرع أيضاً موجودة، ولأن الحكم الشرعي ماترتب على فعله ثواب ، وعلى تركه عقاب ، والمباح ليس كذلك ، فالأباحة ليست بحكم شرعي.
والجواب أن الإباحة حكم شرعى ، لأنه يصدق عليه تعريف الحكم الشرعي، وهو خطاب الله المتعلّق بفعل المكلّف اقتضاء أو تخييراً، والتخيير هو الإباحة .
(۲) والغرض من هذه العبارة : " والإباحة الأصلية نوع منه " دفع سؤال، وهو أن الإباحة الأصلية كانت قبل ورود الخطاب ، فكيف يشملها الخطاب ؟ نعم يشمل الخطاب الإباحة الشرعية . وحاصل الدفع أن الإباحة الأصلية نوع من الإباحة الشرعية ، فالخطاب بالمباح الشرعي هو بعينه الخطاب بالمباح الأصلى ، فكان الخطاب الشرعي أيد الإباحة الأصلية، والمباح قبل الشرع أيضاً ، فاستوعب الخطاب الإباحة الأصلية والشرعية كليهما.
(۳) وقوله : لأن كل ما عدم فيه المدرك الشرعي للحرج الخ
فيه توجيهان : الأول : إنه دليل على أن الإباحة الأصلية نوع من الإباحة الشرعية الأن كل ما لا يكون فيه دليل يُدرك به شرعاً هل في فعله أو تركه حرج؟ فبهذا (أي بعدم وجود الدليل على إدراك الحرج في فعله أو تركه) يُدرك به شرعاً حكم الشارع بالتخيير، أي باختيار الفعل أو الترك، وهذا دليل إباحته شرعاً.
والثاني أن الخطاب على نوعين خطاب خاص و حقيقى وخطاب عام و مجازى، ففي الإباحة الأصلية يوجد الخطاب العام المجازى ، كقوله : دعونى ماتركتكم أي لاتسئلونى فيما لم أمنعكم و لا آمركم وقوله تعالى لا تسئلو عن أشياء ان تبدلكم تسؤكم ) فثبت أن الحكم في الإباحة الأصلية التخيير شرعاً.
(٤) فهى أي الاباحة الأصلية لاتكون ولا تثبت إلا بعد ورود الشرع ، فإن العقل لايكون حاكماً عندنا ، وإن كان الحسن و القبح عقليين ، خلافاً للمعتزلة فإن الحُسن و القبح عندهم عقليان ، وأن العقل حاكم عندهم . وقد تقدم هذا البحث في باب الحسن و القبح
Issue: On ibaha being a legal ruling. Ibaha is a legal ruling because it is the address of the Lawgiver in terms of option (takhyir). And original ibaha (before the Law) is a type of it, because whenever there is no legal evidence (madrak shar'i) for restriction (haraj) in its doing or leaving, that is a legal evidence for the ruling of the Lawgiver of option, so it (ibaha) only exists after the Law, contrary to some Mu'tazila as previously mentioned. Ibaha is a legal ruling because it is the address of the Lawgiver in terms of option, and original ibaha (ibaha before the Law) is a type of it, because whenever there is no legal evidence for restriction in its doing or leaving, that is a legal evidence for the ruling of the Lawgiver of option, so it (ibaha) only exists after the Law, contrary to some Mu'tazila as previously mentioned. And the author mentioned four issues regarding ibaha: this is the first of them. (1) What is the necessity of mentioning this issue: that ibaha is a legal ruling? The detail of the question: There is no doubt that what is investigated in this section are legal rulings, and ibaha is not from them, because it existed before the Law as well, and because a legal ruling is that which has reward for its performance and punishment for its abandonment, whereas the permissible is not like that. So ibaha is not a legal ruling. The answer: Ibaha is a legal ruling because the definition of legal ruling applies to it, namely the address of Allah related to the action of the responsible person in terms of requirement or option, and option is ibaha. (2) The purpose of this statement: "And original ibaha is a type of it" is to rebut a question: that original ibaha existed before the arrival of the address, so how can the address include it? Yes, the address includes legal ibaha. The gist of the rebuttal is that original ibaha is a type of legal ibaha, so the address concerning the legally permissible is precisely the address concerning the originally permissible. Thus the legal address confirmed the original ibaha and what was permissible before the Law as well. So the address encompassed both original and legal ibaha. (3) His statement: "Because whenever there is no legal evidence for restriction..." has two interpretations: First: It is evidence that original ibaha is a type of legal ibaha because whenever there is no evidence through which it is legally known whether there is restriction in its doing or leaving, then by this (i.e., by the absence of evidence for restriction in its doing or leaving) the ruling of the Lawgiver of option is legally known, i.e., choosing to do or leave. This is evidence of its being legally permissible. Second: That address is of two types: specific and real, and general and metaphorical. In original ibaha, there is the general metaphorical address, such as His saying: "Leave me with what I have left you" i.e., do not ask me concerning what I have not prohibited nor commanded you, and His statement: "Do not ask about things which, if disclosed, will distress you." Thus it is established that the ruling in original ibaha is option (takhyir) by law. (4) So it, i.e., original ibaha, only exists and is established after the advent of the Law, for reason is not a judge with us (Hanafis), even though good and evil are rational. Contrary to the Mu'tazila, for good and evil with them are rational and reason is a judge with them. This discussion has preceded in the chapter of good and evil.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This is a dispute (usul al-fiqh) on whether ibaha (permissibility) qualifies as a hukm shar'i (legal ruling). The Hanafi position asserts that it does, against some Mu'tazila who deny it. The author defines hukm shar'i as Allah's address concerning the actions of the mukallaf, either requiring (iqtida') or giving an option (takhyir). Option is precisely ibaha, so ibaha fits the definition. A common objection is that ibaha existed before revelation (e.g., actions in the state of nature), but the author argues that original ibaha is a type of legal ibaha because the absence of a restrictive evidence itself serves as a legal indicator (madrak shar'i) of the Lawgiver's ruling of choice. The commentary provides two interpretations: one from the absence of evidence directly indicating permission, and another from general metaphorical addresses like the Prophet's hadith 'Leave me with what I have left you' and the Quranic verse about not asking about things that might distress. The Hanafi school holds that reason (aql) is not a source of ruling, even though good and evil are rationally known; thus ibaha cannot be established by reason alone, but only after revelation. This contrasts with the Mu'tazila who consider reason as independent for rulings of good and evil.

Key Terms
حكم شرعي (hukm shar'i): The address of Allah related to the actions of the responsible person (mukallaf) in terms of requirement (iqtida') or option (takhyir).
إباحة (ibaha): The ruling of option (takhyir) between doing and leaving an act, considered a legal ruling.
مدرك شرعي (madrak shar'i): A legal indicator or source of evidence that leads to the knowledge of the Lawgiver's ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the definition of a legal ruling (hukm shar'i) that allows ibaha to be included?
A) It is only what is required with reward and punishment.
B) It is the address of Allah related to the mukallaf's action either by requirement or option.
C) It is what is established by reason before revelation.
D) It is only what is derived from explicit textual commands.

Key Takeaway
Ibaha is a hukm shar'i because the definition includes takhyir, and the absence of restrictive evidence serves as a legal indicator of permission.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Refuting the Claim That Mubah Is Wajib
Refuting al-Ka'bi's argument that mubah is wajib

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical structure of al-Ka'bi's argument that mubah is wajib and explain the Hanafi response by denying the minor premise.

Core Idea
Mubah is not wajib; al-Ka'bi's syllogism fails because its minor premise is false.

In this lesson, we examine a rational dispute (ikhtilāf 'aqlī) between the Hanafi school and the Mu'tazilī scholar al-Ka'bi over the nature of mubāḥ. Al-Ka'bi argued that every mubah act is actually wājib on the grounds that leaving it would be unlawful. The Hanafis refute this by carefully analyzing the logical structure of his argument and showing that his minor premise is not universally true.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State the Hanafi view that mubah is not wajib (it is a distinct category with option) and al-Ka'bi's opposing view that mubah is wajib.
This is the foundational ikhtilāf.
2
Reconstruct al-Ka'bi's syllogism
Break down his argument: Major premise – every unlawful leaving is wajib. Minor premise – every mubah, leaving it is unlawful. Conclusion – mubah is wajib.
Recognize the logical form: a standard syllogism.
3
Locate the Hanafi point of attack
The Hanafis deny the minor premise: 'الصغرى ممنوعة' – the minor premise is rejected.
This is the key analytical move.
4
Understand the first counter-argument
The unlawfulness of leaving a mubah act may cease because the requisite (e.g., specific intention) for prohibition is absent. The non-existence of the cause leads to non-existence of the effect.
Appeals to the principle: 'علة العدم عدم علة الوجود'.
5
Understand the second counter-argument and the exception
A mubah act only counts as 'leaving' if one intends by doing it to leave the mubah; this is not inherent. However, if one intends a haram and uses the mubah act to avoid it, then it becomes wajib – and Hanafis accept that exception.
The exception confirms the rule, not al-Ka'bi's generalization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۲۱ :مسألة عدم وجوب المباح
والرد على من قال بوجوبه
المباح ليس بواجب، خلافاً للكعبي، واحتج بأن كل مباح ترك حرام وكل ترک حرام واجب ولو مخيراً
قلنا: الصغرى ممنوعة، أما أولاً فلجواز انعدام الحرام بانعدام المقتضى وهو الإرادة مثلاً، بناء على أن علة العدم عدم علة الوجود، وحينئذ لا يكون عدمه مستنداً إلى فعل المباح الذي هو المانع وأما ثانياً: فلأن فعل المباح إنما يكون تركاً له لوقصد بفعله تركه وذلك لا يلزم نعم لو أراد الحرام ثم قصد بفعل المباح تركه، فإنه يكون واجباً، ونحن نلتزمه، وألزم عليه بأنه مصادمة الإجماع، فأجاب أنه بالنظر إلى ذات الفعل، وهذا بالنظر إلى ما يستلزمه
Issue 21: The issue that mubah is not wajib, and the refutation of those who say it is wajib. Mubah is not wajib, contrary to al-Ka'bi. He argued that every mubah, leaving it is unlawful, and every unlawful leaving is wajib, even if optionally. We say: The minor premise is denied. First, because it is possible that the unlawful ceases to exist due to the absence of the requisite, which is intention for example, based on the principle that the cause of non-existence is the non-existence of the cause of existence. And then its non-existence is not attributed to the act of mubah which is the preventer. Second, because the act of mubah is only a leaving of it if it intends by its act to leave it, and that is not necessary. Yes, if he intended the unlawful and then intended by the mubah act to leave it, then it becomes wajib, and we accept that. And he was objected that it contradicts consensus, so he answered that this is with regard to the essence of the act, and that is with regard to what it necessitates.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute (ikhtilāf) between the Hanafi school and al-Ka'bi (a Mu'tazili scholar) regarding the ruling of mubāḥ (permissible). Al-Ka'bi argued that mubah is actually wājib (obligatory) on the grounds that leaving any mubah act is obligatory (since leaving a mubah act is a specific action that one must do if the act is to be left, and every leaving is a ḥarām (unlawful) act that must be avoided, thus making the mubah act itself wajib to avoid the unlawful leaving). The Hanafi response refutes this by denying the minor premise (al-ṣughrā) that 'every mubah, leaving it is unlawful'. First, the unlawful (al-ḥarām) may cease to exist simply because the requisite (such as intention) for its prohibition is absent; the non-existence of the unlawful is not necessarily caused by the mubah act. Second, the act of mubah only becomes a 'leaving' of the mubah if one specifically intends by performing it to leave that mubah act; but that is not inherent in the mubah act itself. However, if someone intends to commit a ḥarām act but then performs a mubah act with the specific intention to avoid the ḥarām, then that mubah act becomes wajib, and the Hanafis accept that. A further objection that this contradicts consensus is addressed by distinguishing between the essence of the act and its implications.

Key Terms
الصغرى (al-ṣughrā): The minor premise in a syllogism: here, the claim that 'every mubah, leaving it is unlawful' is the minor premise in al-Ka'bi's argument.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is al-Ka'bi's argument for claiming mubah is wajib?
A) Mubah is wajib because the Lawgiver commands it.
B) Every mubah, leaving it is unlawful, and every unlawful leaving is wajib, so mubah is wajib.
C) Mubah is wajib because it is part of the genus of wajib.
D) Mubah is wajib because it leads to reward.

Key Takeaway
Logical disputes in usul al-fiqh are resolved by scrutinizing the premises; here, al-Ka'bi's argument fails because his minor premise is not universally valid.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Refuting the Obligation of Mubah
Refutes Mu'tazili claim that every mubah is wajib

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Mu'tazili syllogism that mubah is wajib, state the Hanafi refutation, and evaluate the strength of each premise.

Core Idea
Mubah is not wajib because the link between mubah and leaving haram is not necessary.

This passage presents a classical usul dispute between the Hanafi school and the Mu'tazili scholar al-Ka'bi over the legal nature of mubah (permissible acts). Al-Ka'bi argued that every mubah is wajib because performing a mubah necessarily entails leaving a haram act, and leaving haram is obligatory. The Hanafi response carefully dismantles this syllogism by challenging its minor premise, showing that the relationship between mubah and leaving haram is contingent, not inherent. Understanding this refinement is crucial for correctly classifying acts in the fivefold taxonomy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mu'tazili syllogism
State the three-step argument: (1) every mubah is a leaving of haram, (2) every leaving of haram is wajib, (3) therefore mubah is wajib (even if optional).
This is a qiyas (analogy) rejected by the Hanafis.
2
Locate the Hanafi rebuttal strategy
The Hanafi response denies the minor premise (الصغرى) — that every mubah is a leaving of haram. Two reasons are given.
The first reason is that haram may be absent due to absence of its cause, not due to mubah.
3
Analyze the first refutation (cause vs. preventer)
If the cause of haram (e.g., intention) is absent, then haram is absent regardless of mubah. The absence is attributed to the missing cause, not to the mubah act.
This uses the principle: 'The cause of non-existence is the non-existence of the cause of existence.'
4
Analyze the second refutation (intention requirement)
Performing mubah does not automatically mean one intends to leave haram. The act of leaving haram only occurs if the mukallaf specifically intends it. Otherwise, mubah may be done without any relation to haram.
If one does intend to leave haram by doing mubah, then that specific act becomes wajib — the Hanafis accept that.
5
Evaluate the consequence and cross-madhhab implications
Consider the objection: if mubah is wajib, then haram would also be wajib (because every haram entails leaving its opposite haram). Al-Ka'bi could accept that from two aspects. Reflect on why Hanafi definitional distinction (ja'iz al-fi'l wa al-tark vs. lazim al-fi'l) is simpler and avoids such absurdities.
This demonstrates the Hanafi preference for direct definitional categories over forced analogies.

Issue: The non-obligation of mubah and the refutation of those who say it is obligatory. Mubah is not obligatory, contrary to al-Ka'bi. He argued that every mubah is a leaving of haram, and every leaving of haram is obligatory, even if optional. We said: The minor premise is denied. First, because it is possible for haram to be absent due to the absence of its requisite, such as intention, based on the principle that the cause of non-existence is the non-existence of the cause of existence. In that case, its non-existence is not attributed to the act of mubah that is the preventer. Second, because the act of mubah is only a leaving of it if one intends by his act to leave it, and that is not necessary. Yes, if he intended haram and then intended to leave it by doing mubah, then it would be obligatory, and we accept that. And it was objected to him that it contradicts consensus, so he answered that it is with respect to the essence of the act, and this is with respect to what it entails. And it was rebutted that it would entail that every haram is obligatory because every haram is a leaving of another haram, its opposite. And he answered that he can accept it considering two aspects. And this is a third issue concerning mubah, where the author refutes those who say... He said: Mubah is not obligatory according to the majority of Ahl al-Sunna, because mubah is permissible in act and omission, and wajib is required in act and prohibited from omission, so it is impossible to combine permission of omission and its prohibition in one thing. Contrary to al-Ka'bi al-Mu'tazili, for he said mubah is obligatory. Al-Ka'bi argued: Every mubah is a leaving of haram, and every leaving of haram is obligatory, even if optional. So mubah is obligatory even if optional, i.e., even if it is a voluntary obligatory, entailing the division of wajib into voluntary and non-voluntary. It was said the meaning of voluntary is that the mukallaf has a choice in leaving haram by engaging in mubah, mandub, or makruh tanzihi. We said in response to al-Ka'bi's argument: The minor premise, that every mubah is a leaving of haram, is denied for two reasons. First, it is possible for haram to be absent due to the absence of its requisite, which is intention, for example. So we do not accept that the leaving of haram came about because of the act of mubah; rather, it is possible that the leaving of haram is due to the absence of the requisite, i.e., the absence of intention for that haram act. This is based on the rational principle that the cause of non-existence (the non-existence of a thing) may be the non-existence of the cause of existence (the existence of that thing). So here, the leaving of haram came about due to the absence of the cause of existence of haram, which is the intention of haram. In that case, if the requisite and cause for the existence of haram is intention, and the cause for the non-existence of haram is the absence of intention, then the non-existence of haram is not attributed to the act of mubah that is the preventer of the existence of haram; rather, the cause of the non-existence of haram is the absence of intention. Second, the act of mubah is only a leaving of it (the haram) if one intends by his act to leave it (leaving haram). And that (the act of mubah for the purpose of leaving haram) is not necessary from the act of mubah; for sometimes permissible acts are done without any thought of leaving haram. Yes, this is possible, if one intends to do...
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage presents a usul dispute: whether mubah (permissible) is wajib (obligatory). The Mu'tazili al-Ka'bi argues that every mubah entails leaving a haram, and leaving haram is obligatory, so mubah is obligatory (though optional). The Hanafi response denies the minor premise (that every mubah is a leaving of haram) on two grounds. First, the absence of haram may be due to the absence of its cause (e.g., lack of intention), not due to performing mubah. Second, even if a person does a mubah act, it does not necessarily imply an intention to leave haram; it could be done without any such purpose. However, if someone specifically intends to leave haram by doing mubah, then that particular act becomes obligatory, and the Hanafis accept that. The text also records a rebuttal: if every mubah is wajib, then every haram would also be wajib (since it entails leaving its opposite haram); al-Ka'bi could accept that from two perspectives. The passage concludes with the majority Sunni position: mubah is defined as permissible both to do and omit, wajib as requiring the act and prohibiting omission, so they are mutually exclusive.

Key Terms
المباح (al-mubah): That which is permissible to do and omit (ja'iz al-fi'l wa al-tark)
الواجب (al-wajib): That which is required in act and prohibited from omission (lazim al-fi'l wa mamnu' al-tark)
الصغرى (al-sughra): The minor premise in a syllogism; here 'every mubah is a leaving of haram'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi response deny that every mubah is a leaving of haram?
A) Because mubah is defined as equal in act and omission, so it cannot be wajib.
B) Because haram might be absent due to lack of intention, not due to the mubah act.
C) Because al-Ka'bi is a Mu'tazili and his opinion is rejected.
D) Because every mubah is actually a mandub.

Key Takeaway
Mubah is not wajib because the relation between mubah and leaving haram is contingent on the presence of intention and the absence of other causes.

Can Mubah Become Wajib to Avoid Haram? A Hanafi Response to al-Ka'bi
Refutation of al-Ka'bi's consensus argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on mubah becoming wajib when it is a means to avoid haram, identify al-Ka'bi's consensus objection, and apply the Hanafi distinction between essence and necessary consequence.

Core Idea
The means to a wajib is wajib — even a permissible act becomes obligatory if it is the only way to avoid the forbidden.

This passage addresses a fundamental usul question: can a mubah (permissible) act become wajib (obligatory) when it serves as a means to avoid haram? The Hanafi school affirms this based on the principle that the means to an obligation is itself obligatory. Al-Ka'bi, a Mu'tazili scholar, objects citing the consensus that acts are divided into five mutually exclusive categories, making it impossible for mubah to become wajib. The text elaborates the Hanafi response, distinguishing between the essence of an act and its necessary consequences, and then deals with a counter-objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling in question
The core issue: can a mubah act become wajib if it is performed specifically to avoid a haram? Note the Hanafi position that it does become wajib.
The text begins by asserting: 'إنه يكون حينئذ واجباً'.
2
Understand al-Ka'bi's proof
Al-Ka'bi argues that consensus divides actions into five distinct categories; mubah and wajib are opposites, so mubah cannot be wajib.
The objection is based on 'الإجماع على أن فعل المكلف ينقسم إلى خمسة أقسام متبائنة'.
3
Analyze the Hanafi distinction
The Hanafi response: the mubah remains mubah in essence (dhat), but becomes wajib by its necessary consequence (lazim) of avoiding haram. This preserves the consensus because the wajib attribute is not part of the definition of mubah.
The text says: 'المباح واجباً بالنظر إلى ما يستلزمه المباح، وهو ترك الحرام'.
4
Follow the counter-objection
A third argument claims that if mubah can be wajib via consequence, then every haram act would also be wajib because it avoids another haram (e.g., liwat avoids zina).
This is a logical extension to test the principle: 'يلزم أن يكون كلّ حرام واجباً'.
5
Assess the reply and the author's warning
The reply: one can affirm that a haram is wajib from the aspect of avoiding another haram, but it remains haram in itself. The author warns that accepting purely rational distinctions would destroy the entire Shariah classification.
The text ends: 'إذا قبل مثل هذه التأويلات العقلية البحتة لم يبق من الأحكام الشرعية شيئ بحاله'.
